Ruling on Jual in Jihad: When It Is Disliked
Commentary on the ruling of jual when there is fay and when not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on jual (compensation) in jihad, and identify the condition (presence or absence of fay) that determines whether it is disliked.

Core Idea
Jual is disliked only when the treasury has fay; otherwise, necessity makes it permissible.

We often assume that paying someone to fight is always permissible, but Islamic law regulates this based on the state's resources. This lesson examines the Hanafi view on jual—a payment made to participants in jihad—and the key factor that changes its ruling: the presence of fay in the public treasury.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the ruling
The passage discusses 'jual' – a payment given to someone for participating in jihad, such as providing horses or weapons. Determine what act is being ruled upon.
Jual is like a wage or compensation for a service in the context of jihad.
2
State the default ruling
The matn says: 'It is disliked to make a jual if there is fay.' So the default ruling is that jual is disliked (makruh) when the treasury contains fay. What is fay? It is wealth taken from non-Muslims without fighting, like jizya and kharaj.
'Disliked' here means makruh tanzih, not haram.
3
Identify the exception
If there is no fay in the treasury, then jual is not disliked. The passage says 'otherwise no' meaning no dislike. The reason given is that it resembles a wage but there is no need for it when the treasury is sufficient.
The condition for dislike is the existence of fay.
4
Understand the rationale
The commentator explains that if necessity arises (e.g., insufficient treasury or urgent need), then there is no harm in Muslims strengthening each other because it repels greater harm by accepting a lesser harm. This is based on the principle of repelling harm.
This is supported by examples from the Prophet and Umar.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider: The Bayt al-Mal has sufficient fay from jizya. An imam offers extra payment to volunteers for a specific campaign. The ruling: jual is disliked. If the treasury is empty, jual is not disliked.
The ruling changes based on the condition of the treasury.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وكره الجعل إن وجد فيء وإلا (لا أي إن لم يوجد فلا كراهة لأنه يشبه الأجر ولا ضرورة إليه لأن مال بيت المال معد لنوائب المسلمين، وإن دعت الضرورة فلا بأس أن يقوي المسلمون بعضهم بعضاً لأن فيه دفع الضرر الأعلى بإلحاق الأدنى، يؤيده أنه عليه السلام أخذ دروعاً من صفوان وعمر رضي الله عنه كان يغزي الأعزب عن ذي الحليلة ويعطي الشاخص فرس القاعد والجعل - بضم الجيم - ما يجعل للإنسان في مقابلة شيء يفعله والمراد به هنا أن يكلف الإمام الناس بأن يقوي بعضهم بعضاً بالكراع والسلاح وغير ذلك النفقة والزاد والفيء المال المأخوذ من الكفار بغير قتال كالخراج والجزية، وأما المأخوذ بقتال فإنه يسمى غنيمة؛ كذا في فتح القدير وظاهره أنه إذا لم يكن في بيت المال فيء وكان فيه غيره من بقية الأنواع فإنه لا يكره الجعل ولا يخفى ما فيه فإنه لا ضرورة لجواز الاستقراض من مراعاة فرض العين أولى لا ينافي ذلك لأن المراد بالأولى هنا الأرجح في التقديم فحيث كان فرض عين يكون خلافه حراماً .
His saying: (And it is disliked to make a jual if there is fay, otherwise no) meaning if there is no fay, there is no dislike, because it resembles a wage and there is no necessity for it since the treasury is prepared for the emergencies of Muslims. If necessity arises, there is no harm in Muslims strengthening each other because it repels greater harm by accepting lesser harm. This is supported by the fact that the Prophet (peace be upon him) took armor from Safwan, and Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) used to send an unmarried man with a married man and give to the one present the horse of the one sitting. Jual (with a jamma on jim) is what is given to a person in exchange for something he does. Here it means that the Imam obligates the people to strengthen each other with horses, weapons, and other such expenses and provisions. Fay is wealth taken from the disbelievers without fighting, like kharaj and jizya. Wealth taken with fighting is called ghanimah; thus in Fath al-Qadir. Its apparent meaning is that if there is no fay in the treasury but there are other types of wealth, then jual is not disliked. What is in it is not hidden: there is no necessity for borrowing; the observation that 'observing fard ayn takes priority' does not contradict this, because 'priority' here means precedence in consideration; so when it is fard ayn, its opposite is haram.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the matn statement: 'And it is disliked to make a jual if there is fay, otherwise no.' The commentator explains that jual (a payment given to someone to participate in jihad) is disliked if there is fay in the treasury, because it resembles a wage and there is no need for it since Bayt al-Mal funds are designated for such emergencies. However, if there is no fay (or no wealth at all), then there is no dislike, and it is permissible for Muslims to contribute to each other to repel harm. The comment supports this with examples from the Prophet (saw) and Umar (ra). The passage also defines key terms: jual is a payment for a service; fay is non-booty wealth from non-Muslims; ghanimah is booty from fighting. The author notes that if there is no fay but other wealth in the treasury, jual is still not disliked. Finally, a clarification is made about 'priority' (awla) not meaning absolute precedence, but rather recommendation; when jihad becomes fard ayn, withholding support is haram.

Key Terms
الجعل (al-jal): Payment made to someone for participating in jihad, such as hiring horses or arming a fighter.
الفيء (al-fay): Wealth taken from non-Muslims without fighting, e.g., jizya, kharaj.
الغنيمة (al-ghanimah): Booty taken from non-Muslims through fighting.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Muslim state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is jual (compensation for jihad) not disliked according to the Hanafi text?
A) When there is fay in the treasury
B) When there is no fay in the treasury
C) Always disliked
D) Only when the sultan permits

Key Takeaway
Jual in jihad is disliked only when the treasury contains fay; otherwise, it is permissible, especially in necessity.

Definitions and Evidence: Jual, Fay, and Ghanimah
Definitions of key terms and supporting evidence for the ruling on jual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define jual, fay, and ghanimah as used in this Hanafi fiqh passage, and identify the prophetic and caliphal evidence supporting the ruling.

Core Idea
The legality of jual in jihad hinges on the presence of fay; definitions distinguish between war and non-war income.

The passage not only gives a ruling but also defines key terms. Understanding these definitions is crucial because the ruling changes based on the meaning of 'fay'. In this lesson, we dissect the terminology and the prophetic and caliphal examples that support the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Define jual
The text defines jual as what is given to a person in exchange for something he does. Here it specifically refers to the Imam making people strengthen each other with horses, weapons, etc.
Jual is like a wage but not exactly ijara because it's a voluntary contribution.
2
Define fay and ghanimah
Fay is wealth taken from disbelievers without fighting, like kharaj and jizya. Ghanimah is wealth taken with fighting. The distinction is important because the ruling on jual depends on fay specifically.
The text references Fath al-Qadir for this definition.
3
Identify the evidence
The commentator cites two examples: The Prophet took armor from Safwan (a Companion) to equip fighters; and Umar used to send an unmarried man with a married one and give the horse of one who stayed to the one going. These show that when the treasury lacks funds, individuals can be called upon.
These actions are cited as support (ta'yid) for the permissibility of jual when there is no fay.
4
Understand the 'awl' principle
The passage ends with a discussion of 'priority' (awla). It clarifies that 'priority' here means precedence in consideration, not that something is absolutely superior. If jihad becomes fard ayn, then not supporting it is haram.
This clarifies that the permissibility due to necessity does not override the obligation of jihad when it is individually obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وكره الجعل إن وجد فيء وإلا (لا أي إن لم يوجد فلا كراهة لأنه يشبه الأجر ولا ضرورة إليه لأن مال بيت المال معد لنوائب المسلمين، وإن دعت الضرورة فلا بأس أن يقوي المسلمون بعضهم بعضاً لأن فيه دفع الضرر الأعلى بإلحاق الأدنى، يؤيده أنه عليه السلام أخذ دروعاً من صفوان وعمر رضي الله عنه كان يغزي الأعزب عن ذي الحليلة ويعطي الشاخص فرس القاعد والجعل - بضم الجيم - ما يجعل للإنسان في مقابلة شيء يفعله والمراد به هنا أن يكلف الإمام الناس بأن يقوي بعضهم بعضاً بالكراع والسلاح وغير ذلك النفقة والزاد والفيء المال المأخوذ من الكفار بغير قتال كالخراج والجزية، وأما المأخوذ بقتال فإنه يسمى غنيمة؛ كذا في فتح القدير وظاهره أنه إذا لم يكن في بيت المال فيء وكان فيه غيره من بقية الأنواع فإنه لا يكره الجعل ولا يخفى ما فيه فإنه لا ضرورة لجواز الاستقراض من مراعاة فرض العين أولى لا ينافي ذلك لأن المراد بالأولى هنا الأرجح في التقديم فحيث كان فرض عين يكون خلافه حراماً .
His saying: (And it is disliked to make a jual if there is fay, otherwise no) meaning if there is no fay, there is no dislike, because it resembles a wage and there is no necessity for it since the treasury is prepared for the emergencies of Muslims. If necessity arises, there is no harm in Muslims strengthening each other because it repels greater harm by accepting lesser harm. This is supported by the fact that the Prophet (peace be upon him) took armor from Safwan, and Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) used to send an unmarried man with a married man and give to the one present the horse of the one sitting. Jual (with a jamma on jim) is what is given to a person in exchange for something he does. Here it means that the Imam obligates the people to strengthen each other with horses, weapons, and other such expenses and provisions. Fay is wealth taken from the disbelievers without fighting, like kharaj and jizya. Wealth taken with fighting is called ghanimah; thus in Fath al-Qadir. Its apparent meaning is that if there is no fay in the treasury but there are other types of wealth, then jual is not disliked. What is in it is not hidden: there is no necessity for borrowing; the observation that 'observing fard ayn takes priority' does not contradict this, because 'priority' here means precedence in consideration; so when it is fard ayn, its opposite is haram.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the matn statement: 'And it is disliked to make a jual if there is fay, otherwise no.' The commentator explains that jual (a payment given to someone to participate in jihad) is disliked if there is fay in the treasury, because it resembles a wage and there is no need for it since Bayt al-Mal funds are designated for such emergencies. However, if there is no fay (or no wealth at all), then there is no dislike, and it is permissible for Muslims to contribute to each other to repel harm. The comment supports this with examples from the Prophet (saw) and Umar (ra). The passage also defines key terms: jual is a payment for a service; fay is non-booty wealth from non-Muslims; ghanimah is booty from fighting. The author notes that if there is no fay but other wealth in the treasury, jual is still not disliked. Finally, a clarification is made about 'priority' (awla) not meaning absolute precedence, but rather recommendation; when jihad becomes fard ayn, withholding support is haram.

Key Terms
الجعل (al-jal): Payment made to someone for participating in jihad, such as hiring horses or arming a fighter.
الفيء (al-fay): Wealth taken from non-Muslims without fighting, e.g., jizya, kharaj.
الغنيمة (al-ghanimah): Booty taken from non-Muslims through fighting.
بيت المال (bayt al-mal): The public treasury of the Muslim state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the definition of 'fay' according to the passage?
A) Wealth taken from non-Muslims with fighting
B) Wealth taken from non-Muslims without fighting
C) Any government revenue
D) Zakat collected from Muslims

Key Takeaway
Correctly defining fay is essential to applying the ruling on jual; evidence from the Prophet and Umar supports permissibility when fay is absent.

The Obligation of Repelling a Sudden Enemy Attack
Ruling with analogy: defense obligation hierarchy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the cascade of obligation for repelling an enemy attack and apply the condition of ability and knowledge.

Core Idea
Repelling a sudden attack is a collective duty that expands from the attacked town to all Muslims.

When an enemy suddenly attacks a Muslim town, who is obligated to defend? This lesson clarifies that the duty is not individually immediate on every Muslim, but a collective obligation that cascades outward. The Hanafi ruling uses a powerful analogy to the preparation of the deceased to illustrate this graduated responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Define hujum
Understand the technical meaning of hujum: a sudden, unauthorized attack. This triggers the obligation.
The term implies a surprise assault, not a prolonged war.
2
Identify the first obligated group
The inhabitants of the attacked town are the first obligated. If they are sufficient to repel, the duty does not pass further.
Sufficiency is assessed by the ability to defend without external aid.
3
Trace the cascade
If the first group is insufficient or negligent, the obligation expands to those nearby, then to those next, and so on, until it becomes obligatory on all Muslims globally.
This cascade continues until either the attack is repelled or every Muslim is obligated.
4
Apply the condition of ability and knowledge
The obligation only applies if the war lasts long enough for distant Muslims to receive news and travel. Otherwise, it is beyond capacity.
This prevents impossible burdens on those who cannot reach in time.
5
Distinguish from rescuing captives
Rescuing a captive is obligatory on every capable Muslim who knows of it, without the graduated cascade.
This highlights the unique structure of the defense obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السير. والهجوم الإتيان بغتة والدخول من غير استئذان؛ كذا في المغرب. المراد هجومه على بلدة معينة من بلاد المسلمين فيجب على جميع أهل تلك البلدة وكذا من يقرب منهم إن لم يكن بأهلها كفاية، وكذا من يقرب ممن يقرب إن لم يكن ممن يقرب كفاية أو تكاسلوا وعصوا وهكذا إلى أن يجب على جميع أهل الإسلام شرقاً وغرباً كتجهيز الميت والصلاة عليه يجب أولاً على أهل محلته فإن لم يفعلوا عجزاً وجب على من ببلدتهم على ما ذكرنا؛ هكذا ذكروا وكان معناه إذا دام الحرب بقدر ما يصل الأبعدون وبلغهم الخبر وإلا فهو تكليف ما لا يطاق بخلاف إنقاذ الأسير وجوبه على كل متجه من أهل المشرق والمغرب ممن علم. ويجب أن لا يأثم من عزم على الخروج وقعوده لعدم خروج الناس وتكاسلهم أو قعود السلطان أو منعه؛ كذا في فتح القدير.
The Book of Siyar. And hujum is coming suddenly and entering without permission; thus in al-Mughrib. What is meant is his attack on a specific town among the towns of the Muslims. So it is obligatory upon all the inhabitants of that town, and likewise upon those near them if the inhabitants are not sufficient, and likewise upon those near to those near if those near are not sufficient or they are negligent and disobedient, and so on until it becomes obligatory upon all the people of Islam, east and west, like the preparation of the deceased and prayer upon him: it is obligatory first upon the people of his neighborhood; if they do not do it due to inability, then it becomes obligatory upon those in his town, as we mentioned. Thus they mentioned, and its meaning is if the war lasts as long as it takes for the distant ones to arrive and the news reaches them; otherwise, it is an obligation beyond capacity. Unlike rescuing a captive, its obligation is upon every capable person among the people of the east and west who knows of it. And it is necessary that the one who intends to go out is not sinful if he stays due to the people not going out and their laziness, or due to the ruler's sitting or preventing him; thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Book of Siyar (Jihad) explaining the obligation of repelling an enemy attack. It defines hujum as a sudden, unauthorized entry. The ruling establishes a cascade of obligation: first the attacked town's inhabitants must defend; if they are insufficient, then those nearby; and so on until it becomes a collective obligation (fard kifayah) on all Muslims globally. The obligation is conditional: it only applies if the war lasts long enough for distant Muslims to receive news and travel. Otherwise, it would be an impossible burden. The passage analogizes this to the obligation of preparing the deceased (tajhiz al-mayyit), which also starts locally and expands. A key distinction is made with rescuing a captive, which is obligatory on every capable Muslim wherever they are, without this graduated approach. Finally, if a person intends to go out for defense but refrains because others are lazy or the ruler prevents, he is not sinful, as the obligation is collective and his remaining does not cause the obligation to fall entirely.

Key Terms
هجوم (hujum): A sudden, unauthorized entry or attack on a Muslim town by enemies.
تجهيز الميت (tajhiz al-mayyit): The preparation of the deceased for burial, including washing, shrouding, and prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the obligation status of repelling an enemy attack on a specific Muslim town?
A) Fard ayn on every Muslim worldwide immediately
B) Fard kifayah starting with the town's inhabitants and expanding
C) Sunnah mu'akkadah
D) Mubah (permissible)

Key Takeaway
The duty to defend against a sudden attack is a collective obligation that begins locally and expands only when needed, conditioned by ability and knowledge.

Rescuing Captives and Accepting the Call to Rescue
Rulings on pursuing captors and accepting reports about captives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which rescuing captives is obligatory and explain why the report of a mustanfir is accepted even if he is sinful.

Core Idea
Rescue captives until they reach enemy fortresses; accept the mustanfir's report because the news spreads widely.

This passage from al-Dhakhira and al-Bazzaziyya addresses two interconnected issues in jihad: the obligation to pursue captors and the evidentiary acceptance of reports summoning help. Understanding the conditions that trigger or cease this obligation is crucial for applying the ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is pursuing the enemy to rescue captives. The ruling is wajib (fard kifayah) upon those capable.
The obligation is collective, not individual.
2
Determine the conditions for obligation
The obligation depends on location: while captives are in Dar al-Islam, pursuit is mandatory. Once in Dar al-Harb, the obligation continues for women and children until they reach enemy fortresses.
For wealth, the obligation ceases upon entering Dar al-Harb.
3
Apply the condition to different categories
Women and children (dharari) are prioritized over property. Dhimmi captives and their property are treated like Muslim captives.
The same ruling applies to dhimmis as to Muslims.
4
Analyze acceptance of the mustanfir's report
The one who summons (mustanfir) is believed even if sinful, because the news becomes widespread among Muslims immediately. Similarly, the sultan's herald is believed.
This facilitates swift action in emergencies.
5
Note the practical scope
The obligation applies regardless of distance; Dar al-Islam is considered one place for the rescue of a Muslim woman captured in the east.
Based on the fatwa from al-Bazzaziyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الذخيرة : إذا دخل المشركون أرضاً فأخذوا الأموال وسبوا الذراري والنساء فعلم المسلمون بذلك وكان لهم عليهم قوّة كان عليهم أن يتبعوهم حتى يستنقذوهم من أيديهم ما داموا في دار الإسلام، فإذا دخلوا أرض الحرب فكذلك في حق النساء والذراري ما لم يبلغوا حصونهم وجدرهم ويسعهم أن لا يتبعوهم في حق المال وذراري أهل الذمة وأموالهم في ذلك بمنزلة ذراري المسلمين وأموالهم .اهـ. وفي البزازية : امرأة مسلمة سبيت بالمشرق وجب على أهل المغرب تخليصها من الأسر ما لم تدخل دار الحرب لأن دار الإسلام كمكان واحد اهـ ومقتضى ما في الذخيرة أنه يجب تخليصها ما لم تدخل حصونهم وجدرهم. وفي الذخيرة ويستوي أن يكون المستنفر عدلاً أو فاسقاً يقبل خبره في ذلك لأنه خبر يشتهر بين المسلمين في الحال وكذلك الجواب في منادي السلطان يقبل خبره عدلاً كان أو فاسقاً اهـ.
And in al-Dhakhira: If the polytheists enter a land and take wealth and capture the children and women, and the Muslims come to know of that and have power over them, it is incumbent upon them to pursue them until they rescue them from their hands as long as they are in the abode of Islam. If they enter the abode of war, then similarly regarding women and children as long as they have not reached their fortresses and walls. And they are permitted not to pursue them regarding wealth. And the children of dhimmis and their wealth are in that regard like the children of Muslims and their wealth. End. And in al-Bazzaziyya: A Muslim woman captured in the east, it is obligatory upon the people of the west to free her from captivity as long as she has not entered the abode of war, because the abode of Islam is like a single place. End. And the implication of what is in al-Dhakhira is that it is obligatory to free her as long as she has not entered their fortresses and walls. And in al-Dhakhira: It is equal whether the one who summons (al-mustanfir) is just or sinful; his report is accepted in that matter because it is news that becomes widespread among the Muslims immediately. Likewise, the reply regarding the herald of the sultan: his report is accepted, whether he is just or sinful. End.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage from al-Dhakhira and al-Bazzaziyya provides detailed rulings on rescuing captives. The primary obligation is to pursue the enemy and recover captives while they are still within Dar al-Islam. Once they cross into Dar al-Harb, the obligation continues for women and children until they reach the enemy's fortifications. For wealth, it is permissible not to pursue after the enemy enters Dar al-Harb. Dhimmi captives and their wealth are treated like Muslim captives in these rulings. A fatwa from al-Bazzaziyya clarifies that a Muslim woman captured in the east must be rescued by Muslims in the west if she hasn't entered Dar al-Harb, treating all of Dar al-Islam as one territory. The passage also discusses accepting news: the one who summons people to rescue captives (al-mustanfir) is believed even if he is sinful, because the news spreads quickly and becomes well-known. Similarly, the sultan's herald is believed regardless of his justice. This facilitates swift action in emergencies.

Key Terms
ذراري (dharari): Children and young dependents, including women, who are taken captive.
مستنفر (mustanfir): The person who summons or calls people to rescue captives or repel an attack.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Until when is it obligatory to rescue a Muslim woman who has been captured?
A) Until the enemy enters Dar al-Harb
B) Until she reaches the enemy's fortresses and walls
C) Until one year passes
D) Always

Key Takeaway
Rescuing captives is obligatory until they are secured in enemy fortresses, and the call to rescue is accepted based on widespread news, not the caller's character.

The Basic Ruling on Ja'l for Jihad and Its Conditions
Opening ruling on ja'l with evidence from practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ja'l for jihad and identify the conditions that make it disliked or permissible.

Core Idea
Ja'l for jihad is disliked when treasury funds exist, but permissible when necessity demands.

In this passage, the author of Kanz al-Daqaiq explains the ruling on ja'l—a compensatory incentive given to support jihad. The ruling hinges on the availability of public funds (fay') and the presence of necessity. We will examine the textual proof from the Prophet's and 'Umar's practice, and understand the underlying principle of repelling greater harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that ja'l is disliked (makruh) if there is fay' in the treasury. If there is no fay', it is not disliked. Confirm the hukm level: makruh tanzih.
The word 'kareha' indicates dislike, not prohibition.
2
Understand the condition that changes the ruling
The presence of fay' (non-war booty) in bayt al-mal makes ja'l disliked because it resembles a wage for worship and there is no need. Absence of funds and necessity allow it.
Fay' includes kharaj and jizya, as explained.
3
Locate the dalil and its wajh al-dalalah
The author cites the Prophet taking shields from Safwan and 'Umar's practice of equipping a bachelor with a married man's horse. This shows that taking support from others is permissible when needed.
The proof is from practice (Sunnah), not Quran.
4
Apply the principle of repelling greater harm
Ja'l is allowed because it repels the greater harm of abandoning jihad by incurring the lesser harm of offering compensation.
This is a qa'ida fiqhiyya (legal maxim).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وكره الجعل إن وجد فيء وإلا لا أي إن لم يوجد فلا كراهة لأنه يشبه الأجر ولا ضرورة إليه لأن مال بيت المال معد لنوائب المسلمين، وإن دعت الضرورة فلا بأس أن يقوي المسلمون بعضهم بعضاً لأن فيه دفع الضرر الأعلى بإلحاق الأدنى، يؤيده أنه عليه السلام أخذ دروعاً من صفوان وعمر رضي الله عنه كان يغزي الأعزب عن ذي الحليلة ويعطي الشاخص فرس القاعد والجعل - بضم الجيم - ما يجعل للإنسان في مقابلة شيء يفعله والمراد به هنا أن يكلف الإمام الناس بأن يقوي بعضهم بعضاً بالكراع والسلاح وغير ذلك النفقة والزاد والفيء المال المأخوذ من الكفار بغير قتال كالخراج والجزية، وأما المأخوذ بقتال فإنه يسمى غنيمة؛ كذا في فتح القدير وظاهره أنه إذا لم يكن في بيت المال فيء وكان فيه غيره من بقية الأنواع فإنه لا يكره الجعل ولا يخفى ما فيه فإنه لا ضرورة لجواز الاستقراض من مراعاة فرض العين أولى لا ينافي ذلك لأن المراد بالأولى هنا الأرجح في التقديم فحيث كان فرض عين يكون خلافه حراماً. وفي الذخيرة : ثم من كان قادراً على الجهاد بنفسه وماله فعليه أن يجاهد بنفسه وماله قال الله تعالى وجاهدوا في الله حق جهاده [ الحج : ۷۸] وحق الجهاد أن يجاهد بنفسه وماله، ولا ينبغي له في هذه الحالة أن يأخذ من غيره جعلاً. ومن عجز عن الخروج وله مال أن ينبغي يبعث غيره عن نفسه بماله، ومن قدر بنفسه ولا مال له فإن كان في بيت المال مال يعطيه الإمام كفايته من بيت المال فإن أعطاه كفايته لا ينبغي أن يأخذ من غيره جعلاً وإلا فله أن يأخذ الجعل من غيره. قال ركن الإسلام علي السغدي : إذا قال القاعد للشاخص خذ هذا المال فاغز به فإنه ليس باستئجار على الجهاد فأما إذا قال خذه لتغزو به عني فهذا استئجار على الجهاد فلا يجوز، وينبغي أن تكون مسألة الحج على هذا التفصيل. وإذا دفع الرجل إلى غيره جعلاً ليغزو به عنه هل له أن يصرفه في غير الغزو؟ فهو على وجهين: إن قال له أغز بهذا المال عني فليس له صرفه في غيره كقضاء دينه ونفقة أهله كمن دفع إلى آخر مالاً وقال حج به عني، وإن قال اغز به فله صرفه إلى غيره كمن دفع مالاً وقال حج به لأنه ملكه المال وأشار إليه إشارة فله أن لا يأخذ بإشارته كقوله هذه الدار لك فاسكنها وهذا الثوب لك فالبسه كان له أن لا يسكنها ولا يلبسه وفي شرح السير : إن للمدفوع إليه أن يترك بعض الجعل لنفقة عياله على كل حال لأنه لا يتهيأ له الخروج إلا بهذا فكان من الجهاد معنى. وتفرع على الوجهين ما إذا عرض له عارض من مرض أو غيره فأراد أن يدفع إلى غيره أقل مما أخذ ليغزو به فإن كان مراده إمساك الفضل لرب المال فلا بأس به، وإن كان مراده الإمساك لنفسه ففي الوجه الأول لا يملك ذلك لأنه ما ملكه بل أباح له الانفاق على نفسه في الغزو، وفي الثاني يملكه لأن له أن لا يغزو أصلاً؛ كذا في الذخيرة مختصراً.
His saying: (And ja'l is disliked if there is fay', otherwise no) i.e. if fay' is not found, then there is no dislike because it resembles a wage and there is no need for it because the wealth of bayt al-mal is prepared for the calamities of the Muslims. And if necessity calls, then there is no harm in Muslims strengthening each other because in it is repelling the greater harm by incurring the lesser. Supporting this is that he (peace be upon him) took shields from Safwan, and 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) used to send the bachelor on behalf of the married man and give the one departing the horse of the one staying. And al-ja'l (with damm on jim) is what is given to a person in exchange for something he does. What is meant here is that the imam charges the people to strengthen each other with horses, weapons, and other expenses and provisions. And al-fay' is wealth taken from the disbelievers without fighting, like kharaj and jizya. As for that taken with fighting, it is called ghanimah. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And its apparent meaning is that if there is no fay' in bayt al-mal but there are other types of wealth, then ja'l is not disliked. But what is in it is not hidden, for there is no necessity to allow borrowing, and consideration of fard ayn is more appropriate; that does not contradict because what is meant by 'more appropriate' here is the preponderant in precedence. So where there is a fard ayn, its opposite is haram. And in al-Dhakhira: Then whoever is capable of jihad with himself and his wealth, he must fight with himself and his wealth. Allah says: 'And strive in Allah with true striving' (al-Hajj 78). True striving is to fight with himself and his wealth. And it is not appropriate for him in this situation to take ja'l from others. And whoever is unable to go out but has wealth, he should send someone else on his behalf with his wealth. And whoever is capable with himself but has no wealth, then if there is wealth in bayt al-mal, the imam should give him sufficient from bayt al-mal. If he gives him sufficient, he should not take ja'l from others; otherwise, he may take ja'l from others. Rukn al-Islam 'Ali al-Sughdi said: If the one sitting (qa'id) says to the one going (shakhis), 'Take this wealth and go for jihad with it,' then it is not an ijarah (hiring) for jihad. But if he says, 'Take it to go for jihad on my behalf,' then this is ijarah for jihad, and it is not permissible. And the issue of hajj should be on this detail. And if a man gives ja'l to someone to go for jihad on his behalf, does he have the right to spend it on other than jihad? There are two aspects: If he said to him, 'Go for jihad with this wealth on my behalf,' then he may not spend it on anything else, like paying his debt or supporting his family, just as if someone gave money to another and said, 'Perform hajj on my behalf.' If he said, 'Go for jihad with it,' then he may spend it on something else, like if someone gave money and said, 'Perform hajj with it,' because he made him the owner of the money and pointed him in a direction, so he may not follow his instruction, like his saying, 'This house is for you, so live in it,' or 'This cloth is for you, so wear it'; he may choose not to live in it or wear it. And in Sharh al-Siyar: The recipient may set aside some of the ja'l for his family's expenses in any case, because he cannot go out without it, so it is from the meaning of jihad. And based on the two aspects, if an obstacle like illness arises and he wants to give to someone else less than what he took to go for jihad, then if he intends to keep the surplus for the owner of the wealth, there is no harm. If he intends to keep it for himself, then in the first aspect he does not own that because he did not own it but rather it was made permissible for him to spend on himself during the jihad; in the second aspect he owns it because he has the right not to go at all. Thus in al-Dhakhira briefly.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This is a comprehensive passage on ja'l (compensation or reward) in the context of jihad. Ja'l is defined as what the imam compels people to give to support each other with equipment and provisions. It is disliked (makruh) if there is fay' (non-war booty like kharaj) in the treasury because then ja'l resembles a wage and is unnecessary; but if there is no fay' and no funds, then it is not disliked because of necessity. The principle of repelling greater harm by incurring lesser is invoked, supported by the Prophet taking shields from Safwan and 'Umar's practice. The passage then distinguishes between the capable and incapable. A capable person must fight with his own self and wealth and should not take ja'l. The incapable with wealth should send someone else. If capable but poor, the imam should provide from bayt al-mal; if not, he may take ja'l. Rukn al-Islam al-Sughdi clarifies that giving money for jihad is not ijarah unless specified as 'on my behalf'. The final part discusses the recipient's use of ja'l: if given with 'on my behalf', he cannot spend elsewhere; if simply 'go for jihad', he can spend as he wishes, even not go. However, he may always set aside some for his family's expenses.

Key Terms
جعل (ja'l): Compensation or reward given for someone to perform a task, here specifically for jihad support.
فيء (fay'): Wealth taken from non-Muslims without war, such as kharaj and jizya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is ja'l (compensation) for jihad not disliked (makruh)?
A) When there is fay' in the treasury
B) When there is no fay' and necessity demands
C) When the recipient is wealthy
D) When the imam orders it

Key Takeaway
Ja'l for jihad is disliked only when public funds are available; necessity makes it permissible, supported by the Prophet's practice.

Rights and Obligations of the Recipient of Ja'l for Jihad
Details on recipient's rights and restrictions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between two types of ja'l (on my behalf vs. go for jihad) and determine the recipient's rights regarding the use and transfer of the ja'l.

Core Idea
Ja'l given 'on my behalf' is restricted to the donor's purpose; ja'l given without restriction gives the recipient ownership.

The passage continues with detailed rulings on what the recipient of ja'l may do with the compensation. It distinguishes between two intentions of the donor: hiring for jihad on his behalf (which is ijarah and invalid) vs. giving a gift for the purpose of jihad (which is ja'l). The recipient's rights vary accordingly, affecting whether he can spend the ja'l on his family or delegate the jihad to another.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between ja'l and ijarah for jihad
If the donor says 'Take this and go for jihad' it is ja'l (permissible). If he says 'Take this to go for jihad on my behalf', it is ijarah for an act of worship, which is invalid according to Hanafis.
The difference is the intention of substitution vs. empowerment.
2
Analyze the two scenarios for spending the ja'l
If the donor says 'Go for jihad on my behalf', the recipient cannot spend the money on anything else (like family needs) because it is restricted. If the donor says 'Go for jihad' (without 'on my behalf'), the recipient owns the money and may spend it as he wishes, even not go for jihad.
This parallels the ruling of hajj: 'Hajj on my behalf' vs. 'Hajj with this money'.
3
Understand the exception for family expenses
According to Sharh al-Siyar, the recipient may set aside some ja'l for his family's expenses in any case, because he cannot go out without that support.
This overrides the restriction based on necessity.
4
Apply the case of transferring the ja'l
If the recipient becomes ill and wants to give the ja'l to someone else to go for jihad, the ruling depends on the original wording. If 'on my behalf', he cannot keep any surplus; if unrestricted, he may keep the surplus.
This shows the importance of the donor's wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وكره الجعل إن وجد فيء وإلا لا أي إن لم يوجد فلا كراهة لأنه يشبه الأجر ولا ضرورة إليه لأن مال بيت المال معد لنوائب المسلمين، وإن دعت الضرورة فلا بأس أن يقوي المسلمون بعضهم بعضاً لأن فيه دفع الضرر الأعلى بإلحاق الأدنى، يؤيده أنه عليه السلام أخذ دروعاً من صفوان وعمر رضي الله عنه كان يغزي الأعزب عن ذي الحليلة ويعطي الشاخص فرس القاعد والجعل - بضم الجيم - ما يجعل للإنسان في مقابلة شيء يفعله والمراد به هنا أن يكلف الإمام الناس بأن يقوي بعضهم بعضاً بالكراع والسلاح وغير ذلك النفقة والزاد والفيء المال المأخوذ من الكفار بغير قتال كالخراج والجزية، وأما المأخوذ بقتال فإنه يسمى غنيمة؛ كذا في فتح القدير وظاهره أنه إذا لم يكن في بيت المال فيء وكان فيه غيره من بقية الأنواع فإنه لا يكره الجعل ولا يخفى ما فيه فإنه لا ضرورة لجواز الاستقراض من مراعاة فرض العين أولى لا ينافي ذلك لأن المراد بالأولى هنا الأرجح في التقديم فحيث كان فرض عين يكون خلافه حراماً. وفي الذخيرة : ثم من كان قادراً على الجهاد بنفسه وماله فعليه أن يجاهد بنفسه وماله قال الله تعالى وجاهدوا في الله حق جهاده [ الحج : ۷۸] وحق الجهاد أن يجاهد بنفسه وماله، ولا ينبغي له في هذه الحالة أن يأخذ من غيره جعلاً. ومن عجز عن الخروج وله مال أن ينبغي يبعث غيره عن نفسه بماله، ومن قدر بنفسه ولا مال له فإن كان في بيت المال مال يعطيه الإمام كفايته من بيت المال فإن أعطاه كفايته لا ينبغي أن يأخذ من غيره جعلاً وإلا فله أن يأخذ الجعل من غيره. قال ركن الإسلام علي السغدي : إذا قال القاعد للشاخص خذ هذا المال فاغز به فإنه ليس باستئجار على الجهاد فأما إذا قال خذه لتغزو به عني فهذا استئجار على الجهاد فلا يجوز، وينبغي أن تكون مسألة الحج على هذا التفصيل. وإذا دفع الرجل إلى غيره جعلاً ليغزو به عنه هل له أن يصرفه في غير الغزو؟ فهو على وجهين: إن قال له أغز بهذا المال عني فليس له صرفه في غيره كقضاء دينه ونفقة أهله كمن دفع إلى آخر مالاً وقال حج به عني، وإن قال اغز به فله صرفه إلى غيره كمن دفع مالاً وقال حج به لأنه ملكه المال وأشار إليه إشارة فله أن لا يأخذ بإشارته كقوله هذه الدار لك فاسكنها وهذا الثوب لك فالبسه كان له أن لا يسكنها ولا يلبسه وفي شرح السير : إن للمدفوع إليه أن يترك بعض الجعل لنفقة عياله على كل حال لأنه لا يتهيأ له الخروج إلا بهذا فكان من الجهاد معنى. وتفرع على الوجهين ما إذا عرض له عارض من مرض أو غيره فأراد أن يدفع إلى غيره أقل مما أخذ ليغزو به فإن كان مراده إمساك الفضل لرب المال فلا بأس به، وإن كان مراده الإمساك لنفسه ففي الوجه الأول لا يملك ذلك لأنه ما ملكه بل أباح له الانفاق على نفسه في الغزو، وفي الثاني يملكه لأن له أن لا يغزو أصلاً؛ كذا في الذخيرة مختصراً.
His saying: (And ja'l is disliked if there is fay', otherwise no) i.e. if fay' is not found, then there is no dislike because it resembles a wage and there is no need for it because the wealth of bayt al-mal is prepared for the calamities of the Muslims. And if necessity calls, then there is no harm in Muslims strengthening each other because in it is repelling the greater harm by incurring the lesser. Supporting this is that he (peace be upon him) took shields from Safwan, and 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) used to send the bachelor on behalf of the married man and give the one departing the horse of the one staying. And al-ja'l (with damm on jim) is what is given to a person in exchange for something he does. What is meant here is that the imam charges the people to strengthen each other with horses, weapons, and other expenses and provisions. And al-fay' is wealth taken from the disbelievers without fighting, like kharaj and jizya. As for that taken with fighting, it is called ghanimah. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And its apparent meaning is that if there is no fay' in bayt al-mal but there are other types of wealth, then ja'l is not disliked. But what is in it is not hidden, for there is no necessity to allow borrowing, and consideration of fard ayn is more appropriate; that does not contradict because what is meant by 'more appropriate' here is the preponderant in precedence. So where there is a fard ayn, its opposite is haram. And in al-Dhakhira: Then whoever is capable of jihad with himself and his wealth, he must fight with himself and his wealth. Allah says: 'And strive in Allah with true striving' (al-Hajj 78). True striving is to fight with himself and his wealth. And it is not appropriate for him in this situation to take ja'l from others. And whoever is unable to go out but has wealth, he should send someone else on his behalf with his wealth. Commentary
This is a comprehensive passage on ja'l (compensation or reward) in the context of jihad. Ja'l is defined as what the imam compels people to give to support each other with equipment and provisions. Recommended Practices for Banners, Drums, and Commanders in Jihad
Recommendations on military banners, drums, and the leader's attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended (mandub) rulings for banners, symbols, drums, and commander qualities in Hanafi jihad, and identify what is disliked (makruh) and permissible.

Core Idea
Banners, symbols, and drums are recommended for order; bells in enemy territory are disliked.

The Hanafi tradition provides detailed guidance for organizing the Muslim army in jihad, covering banners, symbols, drums, and leadership qualities. These rulings are recommendations (mandub), not obligations, reflecting the importance of discipline and morale. The imam has discretion in implementing them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
Each item—banners, symbols, drums, commander qualities—is a recommended practice (mandub). The phrase 'yanbaghi' (it is befitting) indicates recommendation, not obligation.
The hukm level is sunnah ghayr muakkadah (mandub).
2
Note the specific recommended practices
The liwa (banner) for the imam should be white, the raya (flags) for commanders black. Each group should have a symbol (shi'ar). The imam should choose an auspicious word for the symbol.
These are organizational suggestions.
3
Recognize what is disliked
Bells in dar al-harb are disliked (makruh) because they can reveal the Muslims' location to the enemy. In Islamic lands, they are permissible.
Drums for gathering and preparing are permissible, as they are not for amusement.
4
Understand the imam's discretion
The imam has choice in symbols and colors. He should also personally encourage the troops, moving among the ranks and giving glad tidings of victory.
The commander should be wise: not reckless nor overly cautious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية وينبغي أن تكون ألوية المسلمين بيضاء والرايات سوداء واللواء للإمام والرايات للقواد، وينبغي أن يتخذ لكل قوم شعاراً حتى إذا ضل رجل عن رايته نادى بشعاره وليس ذلك بواجب. والشعار العلامة والخيار إلى إمام المسلمين إلا أنه ينبغي له أن يختار كلمة دالة على ظفرهم بالعدو وبطريق التفول ويكره للغزاة اتخاذ الأجراس في دار الحرب لأنه يدلهم على المسلمين، أما في بلاد الإسلام فلا بأس به، ولا بأس بهذه الطبول التي تضرب في الحرب لاجتماع الناس واستعدادهم للقتال لأنها ليست بطبول لهو . وينبغي أن يكون أمير الجيش بصيراً بأمر الحرب حسن التدبير لذلك ليس ممن يقتحم بهم المهالك ولا مما يمنعهم عن الفرصة، وينبغي للإمام أن يستقبل الصفوف ويطوف عليهم يحضهم على القتال ويبشرهم بالفتح إن صدقوا وصبروا ؛ كذا في الظهيرية مختصراً.
And in al-Zahhiriyya: It is befitting that the banners (alwiyah) of the Muslims be white, and the flags (rayat) black. The liwa (banner) is for the imam, and the rayah (flag) for the commanders. And it is befitting that each group adopt a distinct symbol (shi'ar) so that if a man loses his flag, he calls out his symbol. That is not obligatory. And the symbol is a sign, and the choice is up to the imam of the Muslims, but it is befitting that he choose a word indicating their victory over the enemy, by way of optimism (tafawwul). And it is disliked (yukrah) for the fighters to use bells in dar al-harb because it reveals them to the Muslims [enemy]. As for in the lands of Islam, there is no harm. And there is no harm in these drums that are beaten in war to gather people and prepare them for fighting, because they are not drums of amusement. And it is befitting that the commander of the army be perceptive in matters of war, of good management, not one who leads them into destruction, nor one who prevents them from opportunity. And it is befitting that the imam face the ranks and go around them, urging them to fight and giving them glad tidings of victory if they are truthful and patient. Thus in al-Zahhiriyya briefly.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage from al-Zahhiriyya provides recommended practices (mandubat) for the organization of the Muslim army in jihad. First, banners (liwa) should be white and flags (raya) black, with the liwa reserved for the supreme imam and raya for military commanders. Each group should have a distinctive symbol (shi'ar) to aid identity in battle; this is not obligatory but recommended. The imam should choose an auspicious word for the symbol. Using bells in enemy territory is disliked because it can reveal the Muslims' location, but in Muslim lands it is fine. Drums for gathering and preparing for battle are permissible, as they are not for entertainment. The commander should be wise and balanced, neither reckless nor overly cautious. The imam should personally encourage the troops, moving among the ranks and promising victory conditional on their truthfulness and patience.

Key Terms
لواء (liwa'): The main banner of the imam; white.
شعار (shi'ar): A distinctive symbol or slogan for a group of soldiers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because they reveal the Muslims' location to the enemy
C) Because they are impure
D) Because they distract from prayer

Key Takeaway
Recommended army organization details are mandub; the imam has flexibility in implementation.

Validity of Contract with 'Ighzi bihi 'anni'
Critical commentary on contract validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a valid contract for jihad and an invalid hiring for jihad, and explain the Hanafi interpretation of the phrase 'aghzi bihi 'anni'.

Core Idea
A contract to perform jihad is not a binding hiring; it is a valid gift of wealth.

In this passage, the commentator resolves an apparent contradiction: the phrase 'aghzi bihi 'anni' appears to be a hiring for jihad, which is invalid in the Hanafi school. Yet the author seems to declare the contract valid. How can that be? The answer lies in reinterpreting the contract's nature as a gift rather than a binding ijarah, preserving validity while respecting the rule that worship cannot be hired.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent problem
The phrase 'aghzi bihi 'anni' seems to be an ijarah (hiring) for jihad, which is not permissible because worship is a personal obligation.
2
Recognize the resolution
The commentator explains that the contract is valid not as a hiring but as a transfer of ownership (gift). The recipient is not bound to go for jihad.
3
Understand the consequence
The ‘expenditure of wealth is to him’ means the money becomes the recipient's property. He may choose to keep it, spend it, or even return it, but he is not obligated to perform jihad.
4
Note the cross-madhhab dimension
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'is) allow hiring for certain acts of worship, but Hanafis restrict it. This distinction affects whether the recipient is bound to perform the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فليس له معرفة في غير الغزو ظاهره صحة هذا العقد بقوله «اغز به عني» مع أنه استئجار على الجهاد وهو لا يجوز فعليه أن يكون المراد صحة من حيث إنه يجوز له أن لا يغزو بل يكون مصرف المال إليه.
His saying: (So he has no knowledge of other than jihad) its apparent meaning is the validity of this contract by his saying 'Go for jihad with this on my behalf', even though it is a hiring for jihad, which is not permissible. So the intended meaning must be validity in the sense that it is permissible for him not to go for jihad, but rather the expenditure of wealth is to him.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This short passage critically refines the earlier ruling on ijarah for jihad. The commentator addresses the apparent contradiction in saying 'aghzi bihi 'anni' (go for jihad on my behalf) which seems to be a hiring for jihad (an act of worship), considered invalid. The resolution is that the contract is valid not as a hiring but as a gift of the money to the recipient, who may choose not to go. The phrase 'falis lahu ma'rifatun fi ghayr al-ghazw' suggests that the recipient has no knowledge of other than jihad, so the contract is interpreted as a transfer of ownership with a recommendation, not a binding hiring.

Key Terms
استئجار (isti'jar): Hiring someone to perform an act; here, hiring for jihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a contract with the phrase 'aghzi bihi 'anni' (go for jihad on my behalf) considered valid despite being a hiring for jihad?
A) Because hiring for jihad is always permissible
B) Because it is interpreted as a gift of the money, not a binding hiring
C) Because the Prophet allowed it
D) Because the imam must enforce it

Key Takeaway
The apparent hiring for jihad is reinterpreted as a valid gift, preserving the contract's validity while avoiding the invalidity of ijarah for worship.

Obligation of Jihad and Ja'l Rulings
Rulings on jihad obligation and ja'l contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligation of jihad for a capable person and apply the Hanafi ruling on when a person may take ja'l for jihad.

Core Idea
Jihad is fard on the capable; ja'l is allowed only when the imam fails to provide.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the obligation of jihad and the intricate rulings regarding taking payment (ja'l) for performing jihad. It distinguishes between the capable and the incapable, and between various contractual wordings. Understanding these distinctions is key to applying Hanafi fiqh correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation of jihad
The text states that whoever is capable with person and wealth must perform jihad with both, citing the Quranic verse. This establishes fard ayn on such individuals.
The proof is the imperative 'جاهدوا' which indicates obligation in Hanafi usul.
2
Determine ruling on taking ja'l when capable
If a person is capable with both person and wealth, he should not take ja'l from others. If capable with person only, and the imam provides from the treasury, he should not take ja'l. If the imam does not provide, he may take ja'l.
The phrase 'لا ينبغي' indicates it is not proper, not a prohibition.
3
Distinguish between general and specific ja'l contracts
If someone says 'take this wealth and fight with it', it is not a hire for jihad and is valid. If he says 'take it to fight on my behalf', it becomes a hire for an act of worship and is invalid.
Al-Sughdi's ruling. Applies to Hajj similarly.
4
Analyze the recipient's rights over ja'l money
If the payment is conditional on fighting on behalf of the payer, the recipient cannot spend it on other needs (like debt or family). If unconditional, he may spend it elsewhere because ownership is transferred.
The analogy of 'this house is for you' shows he may ignore the instruction.
5
Examine the case of an obstacle and delegation
If the recipient falls ill and wants to delegate to another with less money, the surplus must go back to the payer in the first scenario (if payment was conditional) but can be kept in the second scenario (if unconditional).
This is because in the first scenario he was only given permission to spend on fighting, not ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السير
من بقية الأنواع ولذا لم يذكر الفيء في الذخيرة والولوالجية إنما ذكر مال بيت المال وهو الحق. وفي الذخيرة : ثم من كان قادراً على الجهاد بنفسه وماله فعليه أن يجاهد بنفسه وماله قال الله تعالى وجاهدوا في الله حق جهاده [ الحج : ۷۸] وحق الجهاد أن يجاهد بنفسه وماله، ولا ينبغي له في هذه الحالة أن يأخذ من غيره جعلاً. ومن عجز عن الخروج وله مال أن ينبغي يبعث غيره عن نفسه بماله، ومن قدر بنفسه ولا مال له فإن كان في بيت المال مال يعطيه الإمام كفايته من بيت المال فإن أعطاه كفايته لا ينبغي أن يأخذ من غيره جعلاً وإلا فله أن يأخذ الجعل من غيره. قال ركن الإسلام علي السغدي : إذا قال القاعد للشاخص خذ هذا المال فاغز به فإنه ليس باستئجار على الجهاد فأما إذا قال خذه لتغزو به عني فهذا استئجار على الجهاد فلا يجوز، وينبغي أن تكون مسألة الحج على هذا التفصيل. وإذا دفع الرجل إلى غيره جعلاً ليغزو به عنه هل له أن يصرفه في غير الغزو؟ فهو على وجهين: إن قال له أغز بهذا المال عني فليس له صرفه في غيره كقضاء دينه ونفقة أهله كمن دفع إلى آخر مالاً وقال حج به عني، وإن قال اغز به فله صرفه إلى غيره كمن دفع مالاً وقال حج به لأنه ملكه المال وأشار إليه إشارة فله أن لا يأخذ بإشارته كقوله هذه الدار لك فاسكنها وهذا الثوب لك فالبسه كان له أن لا يسكنها ولا يلبسه وفي شرح السير : إن للمدفوع إليه أن يترك بعض الجعل لنفقة عياله على كل حال لأنه لا يتهيأ له الخروج إلا بهذا فكان من الجهاد معنى. وتفرع على الوجهين ما إذا عرض له عارض من مرض أو غيره فأراد أن يدفع إلى غيره أقل مما أخذ ليغزو به فإن كان مراده إمساك الفضل لرب المال فلا بأس به، وإن كان مراده الإمساك لنفسه ففي الوجه الأول لا يملك ذلك لأنه ما ملكه بل أباح له الانفاق على نفسه في الغزو، وفي الثاني يملكه لأن له أن لا يغزو أصلاً؛ كذا في الذخيرة مختصراً. وفي الظهيرية وينبغي أن تكون ألوية المسلمين بيضاء والرايات سوداء واللواء للإمام والرايات للقواد، وينبغي أن يتخذ لكل قوم شعاراً حتى إذا ضل رجل عن رايته نادى بشعاره وليس ذلك بواجب. والشعار العلامة والخيار إلى إمام المسلمين إلا أنه ينبغي له أن يختار كلمة دالة على ظفرهم بالعدو وبطريق التفول ويكره للغزاة اتخاذ الأجراس في دار الحرب لأنه يدلهم على المسلمين، أما في بلاد الإسلام فلا بأس به، ولا بأس بهذه الطبول التي تضرب في الحرب لاجتماع الناس واستعدادهم للقتال لأنها ليست بطبول لهو . وينبغي أن يكون أمير الجيش بصيراً بأمر الحرب حسن التدبير لذلك ليس ممن يقتحم بهم المهالك ولا مما يمنعهم عن الفرصة، وينبغي للإمام أن يستقبل الصفوف ويطوف عليهم يحضهم على القتال ويبشرهم بالفتح إن صدقوا وصبروا ؛ كذا في الظهيرية مختصراً.
Book of Siyar: From the remaining types, therefore he did not mention fay' in al-Dhakhira and al-Walwalijiyya; he only mentioned the treasury of Muslims, and that is correct. In al-Dhakhira: Then whoever is capable of jihad with his person and wealth must perform jihad with his person and wealth. Allah said: 'And strive in Allah with due striving' [al-Hajj 78]. And due striving is that he strive with his person and wealth. In this case, it is not proper for him to take a ja'l from others. If one is unable to go out (to battle) and has wealth, he should send another on his behalf with his wealth. If one is capable with his person but has no wealth, then if there is money in the treasury, the imam should give him sufficient provision from the treasury. If he gives him sufficient, he should not take a ja'l from others; otherwise, he may take a ja'l from others. Rukn al-Islam Ali al-Sughdi said: If a stay-at-home says to a departing person, 'Take this wealth and go fight with it,' this is not a hire for jihad. But if he says, 'Take it to go fight on my behalf,' this is a hire for jihad and is not permissible. The issue of Hajj should be on this same detail. If a man gives another a ja'l to go fight on his behalf, does he have the right to spend it on other than fighting? There are two scenarios: If he said, 'Go fight on my behalf with this wealth,' then he may not spend it on other things like paying his debt or his family's expenses, just as if one gave wealth to another and said, 'Perform Hajj on my behalf with it.' If he said, 'Go fight with it,' then he may spend it on other things, just as if one gave wealth and said, 'Perform Hajj with it,' because he made him owner of the wealth and indicated an instruction; he may ignore his instruction, like his saying, 'This house is for you, so dwell in it,' and 'This garment is for you, so wear it' – he may not dwell in it or wear it. In Sharh al-Siyar: The recipient may leave some of the ja'l for his family's expenses in any case, because he cannot go out without it, so it is part of jihad in meaning. Derived from the two scenarios: If an obstacle like illness occurs and he wants to give to another less than what he took so that he may fight with it, if his intent is to retain the surplus for the owner, there is no problem. If his intent is to retain it for himself, then in the first scenario he does not own it because he was not given ownership but permission to spend on himself in fighting; in the second scenario he does own it because he may choose not to fight at all. This is from al-Dhakhira, summarized. In al-Zahiriyya: The banners (alwiya) of the Muslims should be white, and the flags (rayat) black; the banner is for the imam and flags for the commanders. Each group should adopt a distinctive sign so that if a man loses his flag, he calls out by his sign. This is not obligatory. The sign is a signal; the choice is left to the imam of the Muslims, though he should choose a word indicating their victory over the enemy through optimism. It is disliked for warriors to use bells in dar al-harb because it leads the enemy to the Muslims; in Islamic lands there is no problem. There is no problem with the drums beaten in battle to gather people and prepare for fighting, as they are not drums of amusement. The commander of the army should be knowledgeable in warfare, good in planning, neither rushing them into dangers nor preventing them from opportunities. It is recommended for the imam to face the ranks and circulate among them, urging them to fight and giving them glad tidings of victory if they are truthful and patient. This is from al-Zahiriyya, summarized.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, covers several rulings related to jihad. It begins by clarifying that the author of Kanz mentioned 'treasury of Muslims' rather than 'fay''. Then it presents the ruling: jihad is obligatory (fard) on those capable with both person and wealth, citing the Quranic ayah. If capable, one should not take payment (ja'l) from others to perform it. If unable to go oneself but has wealth, one should send another with one's wealth. If capable but without wealth, the imam should provide from the treasury; if provision is given, one takes no ja'l; otherwise, one may take ja'l. The text then quotes Rukn al-Islam al-Sughdi distinguishing between giving money for fighting generally (not a hire) and specifying 'on my behalf' (which is a hire and invalid). The same applies to Hajj. When giving ja'l, if the payer specifies 'on my behalf', the recipient cannot spend on other needs; if the payment is unconditional ('go fight with it'), the recipient can spend elsewhere because ownership is transferred. Even in the first case, the recipient may use some for family expenses as a necessity. If an obstacle arises, one may delegate to another for less; retaining surplus for the payer is fine, but for oneself depends on the initial ownership. Finally, the passage addresses battle symbols: banners white, flags black; each group should have a sign; bells disliked in enemy territory, drums permissible. The commander should be skilled and the imam should encourage the troops.

Key Terms
جعل (ja'l): Payment given to someone to perform a task, here specifically for going to fight or performing Hajj on behalf of another.
كتاب السير (Kitab al-Siyar): The chapter on military expeditions, governance during war, and related rulings in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a person says to another 'Take this wealth and go fight with it' (without specifying 'on my behalf'), what is the ruling?
A) It is an invalid hire (ijara) for jihad.
B) It is a valid ja'l, not a hire, and the recipient may spend it as he wishes.
C) It is a valid hire and the recipient must spend it only on fighting.
D) It is invalid because the intention is unclear.

Key Takeaway
Jihad is fard on the capable, and ja'l is permissible under specific conditions; the contractual wording determines the recipient's rights over the payment.

Battle Practices: Banners, Flags, Drums, and the Commander's Role
Battle practices and leadership etiquette

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sunnah practices regarding battle banners and flags, the ruling on bells and drums, and the recommended conduct of the commander and imam.

Core Idea
Banners white, flags black; drums permissible, bells disliked; the commander is wise and the imam inspires.

This section from Bahr al-Raiq discusses the symbolic and practical aspects of warfare: the colors of banners and flags, the use of distinctive signs, and the permissibility of drums. It also outlines the qualities of a good commander and the imam's duty to motivate the army. These rulings are based on the practice of the Prophet and the early caliphs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended colors for banners and flags
The text recommends that banners (alwiya) be white and flags (rayat) black. The banner is for the imam and flags for commanders.
This is based on the practice of the Prophet and the Rightly Guided Caliphs.
2
Understand the use of the shi'ar (distinctive sign)
Each group should adopt a shi'ar so that if a soldier loses his flag, he can call out by his sign. This is recommended, not obligatory. The imam chooses an optimistic word.
Optional but encouraged for organization.
3
Determine ruling on bells
Bells are disliked in dar al-harb because they reveal Muslim positions. In Muslim lands, there is no problem.
Dislike (makruh) due to disclosure, not intrinsic prohibition.
4
Determine ruling on drums
Drums used for gathering and preparing for battle are permissible because they are not drums of amusement.
Permissible for utilitarian purposes; not for entertainment.
5
Identify the qualities of the commander and the imam's actions
The commander must be knowledgeable in warfare and balanced: not reckless nor overly cautious. The imam should personally review the ranks, urge the troops, and promise victory if they are truthful and patient.
Leadership is both strategic and motivational.

Source Text

شعار (shi'ar): A distinctive emblem or slogan used by a military unit for identification and coordination in battle.
لواء (liwa'): A banner carried by the imam (supreme leader) of the army, distinct from flags (rayat) for commanders.

The Distinction Between Ja'l and Ijara in Jihad
Critical note on ja'l vs ijara in jihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the wording that transforms a valid ja'l contract into an invalid ijara for acts of worship, and state the Hanafi ruling on hiring someone to perform jihad.

Core Idea
Saying 'fight on my behalf' converts a ja'l into an invalid hire (ijara) for worship.

In this lesson, we examine a critical comment from a Hanafi commentary on the ja'l contract for jihad. The matn apparently implies that saying 'fight on my behalf' makes the contract valid, but the commentator flags a contradiction: such wording transforms the gift into a hire (ijara) for an act of worship, which is prohibited in the Hanafi school. By understanding this nuance, you will grasp a key boundary between permissible reward-based giving and invalid hiring for religious obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent ruling in the matn
The matn states that with the phrase 'fight on my behalf', the recipient has no authority to spend elsewhere. This seems to treat the contract as valid and binding.
The matn is likely from Kanz al-Daqa'iq or similar.
2
Note the commentator's objection
The commentator says 'even though it is a hire (istijar) and it has passed that it is not permissible'. He points out that the wording makes it a hire, which is invalid for jihad.
The phrase 'ta'ammal' (reflect) signals a problem that needs resolution.
3
Understand why hire for worship is invalid
In Hanafi fiqh, hiring someone (ijara) to perform an obligatory act of worship is invalid because worship is a personal duty that cannot be transferred for payment. Ja'l, on the other hand, is a permissible reward given without conditions of hire.
The distinction lies in the conditional nature of 'on my behalf'.
4
Apply the principle to the phrase 'fight on my behalf'
The phrase specifies that the payment is for the fighter's action on behalf of the payer. This creates a contractual obligation akin to a wage, making it ijara and thus invalid.
If the payer simply gave the money unconditionally, it would be a valid ja'l.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (فليس له معرفة في غير الغزو ظاهره صحة هذا العقد بقوله «اغز به عني» مع أنه استئجار وقد مر أنه لا يجوز تأمل
His saying: 'he has no authority to spend in other than fighting' – its apparent meaning is the validity of this contract by his saying 'fight on my behalf with it', even though it is a hire (istijar) and it has passed that it is not permissible. Reflect.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This is a brief critical note from the commentator (perhaps al-Haskafi or another) on a statement in the matn. The matn apparently said that if one says 'fight on my behalf,' the recipient has no authority to spend the ja'l elsewhere. The commentator points out that this wording implies the contract is valid, but earlier it was established such a contract is a hire (istijar) for an act of worship, which is invalid. The commentator says 'ta'ammal' (reflect), indicating an inconsistency between the matn's apparent ruling and the established principle. The lesson is that the wording 'on my behalf' makes it a hire and thus not permissible, so the matn's ruling needs careful interpretation.

Key Terms
استئجار (istijar): A contract of hire (ijara) for a service; here specifically hiring someone to perform jihad, which is invalid because acts of worship cannot be hired out.
جعل (ja'l): A prize or reward given conditionally but without the contractual obligations of hire; permissible for voluntary acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the critical note, why does the phrase 'fight on my behalf' (اغز به عني) render the contract problematic?
A) Because it makes the payment a gift (hiba).
B) Because it turns the ja'l into a hire (istijar) for jihad, which is invalid.
C) Because the recipient must spend it on his family.
D) Because it makes the payer responsible for sins.

Key Takeaway
A ja'l for jihad is valid only if the payment is unconditional; specifying 'on my behalf' makes it a hire and invalid.

Kitabi Conversion: The Requirement of Tabarru' and Entry Declaration
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditions for Kitabi conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on Kitabi conversion, distinguish between the two views within the Hanafi school, and apply the relied-upon ruling to a modern conversion scenario.

Core Idea
A Kitabi's shahada alone does not make him Muslim today; he must also say: 'I disavow my religion and enter Islam.'

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses a subtle but critical fiqh issue: what constitutes embracing Islam for a Jew or Christian in our time? While the Prophet ﷺ accepted the shahada alone, Imam Muhammad ruled that contemporary Kitabis must explicitly disavow their previous faith and declare entry into Islam. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this position and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being judged: the conversion of a Kitabi. What is required for this conversion to be valid according to the Hanafi school?
The dispute is over whether the shahada alone is sufficient or an additional declaration is needed.
2
State the Hanafi position (Imam Muhammad)
The relied-upon position: the Kitabi must say 'tabarra'tu min dini wa dakhaltu fi dini al-Islam' (I disavow my religion and enter Islam) along with the shahada. Explain why: to remove ambiguity about their belief in Muhammad's universality.
This is the view of Imam Muhammad and later Hanafi masters.
3
Identify the opposing view and its basis
Some Hanafi scholars (e.g., the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) imply the shahada suffices, as it did in the Prophet's time. Their proof is the general command to fight until people say 'La ilaha illa Allah'.
This view is weaker and not relied upon for fatwa.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and thamarah al-khilaf
The hadith applies to those who deny tawhid or prophethood entirely, but Kitabis acknowledge Allah and restrict prophethood. The additional declaration removes any pretence. Practical consequence: without it, the Kitabi is not treated as Muslim for marriage, inheritance, etc.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is significant in daily fiqh.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its modern application
The mufta bihi is the position of Imam Muhammad: tabarru' and entry declaration are required. A modern Kitabi converting must say both the shahada and the additional declaration.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His saying: 'Then if we besiege them, we call them to Islam' – meaning we tighten upon the disbelievers and surround them. It is said: the enemy besieged him, etc. So we demand from them entry into the religion of Islam. Because Imam Ahmad narrated from Ibn Abbas that he said: The Prophet never fought a people except that he called them [first]. And in the Sahih: I have been commanded to fight people until they say 'La ilaha illa Allah'; if they say it, they protect their blood and wealth from me except by its due, and their account is with Allah. The author did not mention what makes a disbeliever become Muslim – it is of two types: speech and action. Disbelievers are categories: a category that denies the Exalted Creator – their Islam is by acknowledging His existence; a category that acknowledges Him but denies His oneness – their Islam is by acknowledging His oneness; a category that acknowledges His oneness but denies the messengerhood of Muhammad – their Islam is by acknowledging his messengerhood. The principle is that whoever acknowledges the opposite of what was known of his belief is judged to be Muslim. This is for other than the Kitabi. As for the Jew and Christian, in his time (peace be upon him) their Islam was through the two testimonies (shahada), because they denied the Prophet's messengerhood. But today in the lands of Iraq, one is not judged a Muslim by them unless he says: 'I disavow my religion and I enter into Islam', because they say he was sent to Arabs and non-Arabs, not to the Children of Israel. This is explicitly stated by Muhammad (rahimahullah). He stipulated along with disavowal their declaration of entering Islam because one may disavow Judaism and enter Christianity or Zoroastrianism. If it is said to a Christian: 'Is Muhammad the Messenger of Allah?' – he does not become Muslim, and this is the correct view. If he says: 'He is a messenger to Arabs and non-Arabs,' he does not become Muslim, because he could say he is a messenger to Arabs and non-Arabs but he has not been sent yet. If it is said: It must be that the Jew or Christian is not judged Muslim even if he acknowledges Muhammad's messengerhood, disavows his religion, and enters Islam, unless he believes in Allah, His angels, books, messengers, the resurrection, and destiny (good and evil from Allah), because these are conditions of Islam as in the Hadith of Jibril. We say: Acknowledgment of these things, even if not found explicitly, is found by implication, because when he acknowledges his entry into Islam, he has committed to all that is a condition of its validity. If a Kitabi says 'I am Muslim' or 'I have become Muslim,' he is not judged Muslim because they claim that for themselves. Similarly, if he says 'I am on the religion of Hanifiyya.' If a dhimmi says to a Muslim 'I am Muslim' [he is not?]. This is in al-Dhakhira and al-Fatawa. The conclusion is that a Kitabi today, if he utters the two testimonies, is not judged Muslim. In al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya: He was asked if he said: 'A Muslim like you is true' and he said 'Yes.'
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq deals with the requirements for calling enemies to Islam before battle and the conditions for a Kitabi (Jew or Christian) to become Muslim. The first part cites hadith that the Prophet would call people to Islam before fighting; it is an obligation (wajib) to invite them first. Then it discusses the categories of disbelievers and how each becomes Muslim by acknowledging the opposite of their denial. For Kitabis, it explains a significant Hanafi position: in the time of the Prophet, they became Muslim by uttering the shahada because they only denied prophethood. However, in later times (specifically in Iraq), due to confusion in their beliefs, the shahada alone is insufficient. They must additionally say: 'I disavow my religion and I enter into Islam' (tabarru' and declaration of entry). This is the position of Imam Muhammad as explicitly stated. The reason is that they might affirm the prophethood but restrict it to Arabs, or they might disavow Judaism but adopt another false religion. Even then, they are not required to explicitly mention all articles of faith (angels, destiny, etc.) because declaring entry into Islam implies acceptance of all. The text also mentions that simply saying 'I am Muslim' by a Kitabi is not accepted because they often claim that falsely. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute: the author of Kanz seems to have not mentioned this condition, and the commentator (perhaps from al-Dhakhira) and others insist on it. The passage ends with a query from al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya: if a dhimmi says 'A Muslim like you is true' and the Muslim says 'yes' – incomplete. The overall conclusion is that the shahada alone does not make a Kitabi Muslim in the current context.

Key Terms
تبري (tabarru'): Disavowal or renunciation of one's previous religion; a required statement for a Kitabi converting to Islam in later times according to Imam Muhammad.
كتابي (Kitabi): A Jew or Christian; a member of a revealed religion with a scripture (Tawrat or Injil). In Hanafi fiqh, they have special status for jizya and conversion.
شهادتين (shahadatain): The two testimonies: 'La ilaha illa Allah, Muhammadun Rasul Allah'. In this text, it is debated whether uttering them suffices for a Kitabi's conversion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They must simply say 'I am Muslim'.
B) They must say 'I disavow my religion and I enter into Islam'.
C) They must recite a verse from the Quran.
D) They must perform a ritual bath (ghusl).

Key Takeaway
A Kitabi today must not only utter the shahada but also explicitly disavow his previous religion and declare entry into Islam; the shahada alone does not suffice in the relied-upon Hanafi position.

The Obligation of Da'wah and the Rules of Jizya
Ruling with proof on da'wah and jizya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on da'wah before fighting and identify which non-Muslims may pay jizya.

Core Idea
Before any fight, the call to Islam is wajib; jizya is only accepted from certain non-Muslims, not Arab polytheists.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies two interconnected obligations in jihad: the duty to call non-Muslims to Islam (da'wah) before hostilities, and the acceptance of jizya from those who refuse but are eligible. Understanding these rules is essential for applying the Hanafi position on warfare and the treatment of non-Muslim subjects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation of da'wah
Locate the author's statement that fighting is not permissible until the call to Islam has been given. Note that this is based on the Prophet's instruction to army commanders.
The imperative 'ud'u' indicates wujub.
2
Recognize categories for jizya
Identify that Arab polytheists and apostates are excluded from jizya; they must accept Islam or face the sword. Other non-Muslims (e.g., People of the Book, Zoroastrians) may pay jizya.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Understand the effect of accepting jizya
Once jizya is accepted, the non-Muslims become dhimmis with rights and duties similar to Muslims in civil matters, except for specific prohibitions like wine and pork.
Dhimma is established by mere acceptance, no separate contract needed.
4
Apply the conversion principle
Note that a person who utters the shahada voluntarily, even without explicit disavowal of his former religion, becomes Muslim. This is the operative Hanafi view in later fatwa practice.
Imam Muhammad's requirement of tabarri was specific to his context.
