قوله : (فإن أسلموا وإلا إلى (الجزية أي وإن لم يسلموا ندعوهم إلى أداء الجزية للحديث المعروف، وسيأتي التصريح من المصنف أن مشركي العرب والمرتدين لا تقبل منهم الجزية بل إما الإسلام أو السيف فلا يدعو إليها ابتداء لعدم الفائدة فلا يرد على إطلاقه هنا. وفي شرح الطحاوي : إذا أسلموا نترك أموالهم ونجعل أراضيهم عشرية ونأمرهم بالتحول من دارهم إلى دار الإسلام لأن المقام للمسلم في دار الحرب مكروه، فإن أبوا أخبرهم أنهم كأعراب المسلمين ليس لهم في الفيء ولا في الغنيمة ولا في الخمس ولا في بيت المال نصيب. هذا إذا كان مكانهم بدار الحرب ليس متصلاً بدار الإسلام، فإن كان متصلاً لا يؤمرون بالتحول. وفي التتارخانية : وينبغي للإمام أن يبين لهم مقدار الجزية ووقت وجوبها ويعلمهم أنه إنما يأخذها في كل سنة مرة وأن الغني يؤخذ منه كذا ومن الفقير كذا ومن الوسط كذا اهـ. قوله : (فإن قبلوا فلهم ما لنا وعليهم ما علينا أي قبلوا إعطاء الجزية صاروا ذمة لنا قال علي رضي الله عنه : إنما بذلوا الجزية لتكون دماؤهم كدمائنا وأموالهم كأموالنا. وسيأتي في البيوع استثناء عقدهم على الخمر والخنزير وأن عقدهم على الخمر كعقدنا على العصير، وعقدهم على الخنزير كعقدنا على الشاة، وقدمنا أن الذمي مؤاخذ بالحدود والقصاص إلا حد شرب الخمر، وتقدم في كتاب النكاح أنهم إذا اعتقدوا جوازه بغير مهر أو شهود أو في عدة تتركهم وما يدينون بخلاف الربا فإنه مستثنى من عقودهم قوله : (ولا نقاتل من لا تبلغه الدعوة إلى الإسلام) أي لا يجوز القتال لقوله عليه الإسلام في وصية أمراء الأجناد «فادعهم إلى شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله ولأنهم بالدعوة يعلمون أنا نقاتلهم على الدين لا على سلب الأموال وسبي
منهم
بعموم
مجهولاً كما إذا أتى إلى مسلم وقال له أعرض علي الإسلام فلقنه الشهادتين وأتى بهما طائعاً مختاراً وكذا ما جرت به العادة في زماننا من أنه يذهب إلى المحكمة ويسلم عند القاضي فهذا لا شك ولا ريب في اسلامه وإن لم يصرح بالتبري والعدول عما ورد مع الأدلة الصريحة بمجرد هذا الاحتمال نبذ للشريعة بالكلية فإن الإمام محمداً رحمه الله تعالى لم يشترط التبري إلا لتيقنه وعلمه بحال أهل بلاده واعتقادهم تخصيص البعثة بغير بني إسرائيل، ولولا علمه ذلك منهم لم يسخ له ولا لمن بعده مخالفة ما وردت به الشريعة من الاكتفاء بالشهادتين فيجب إدارة الحكم على علته في كل زمان ولذا قالوا لا يحل لأحد أن يفتي بقولنا حتى يعلم من أين قلنا فاغتنم هذا التحرير الفريد وما مشى عليه المؤلف هنا تبعاً لقارئ الهداية ذكر العلائي في شرحه على الملتقى في الردة أنه أفتى به صنع الله أفندي في فتاويه وأنه أفتى به ابن كمال باشا وأنه ذكر في شرح الملتقى لداماد أفندي أنه المعمول به . قوله : (صاروا (ذمة (لنا قال الرملي : يدل على أنه بمجرد القبول يصيرون ذمة من غير عقد ودعاؤنا قبل كافٍ، ويدل أيضاً على أن الإمام ليس له الامتناع من اتخاذهم
And upon them is what is upon us, and we do not fight those whom the call to Islam has not reached, and we call (recommended) those whom it has reached, otherwise. His statement: (If they accept Islam, otherwise to the jizya) meaning if they do not accept Islam, we call them to pay the jizya due to the well-known hadith. And the author will explicitly mention that Arab polytheists and apostates do not have jizya accepted from them, but rather either Islam or the sword, so he does not call them to it initially because there is no benefit, so it does not reflect on its absolute here. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: If they accept Islam, we leave their wealth and make their lands 'ushriyya, and we order them to move from their abode to the abode of Islam because residing for a Muslim in the abode of war is disliked. If they refuse, we inform them that they are like the Bedouin Muslims, they have no share in the fay', ghanima, khums, or bayt al-mal. This if their place is in the abode of war not connected to the abode of Islam; if it is connected, they are not ordered to move. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya: The imam should explain to them the amount of jizya and its time of obligation, and inform them that he takes it once every year, and that from the rich such-and-such, from the poor such-and-such, from the middle such-and-such. His statement: (If they accept, then for them is what is for us and upon them what is upon us) meaning they accept giving the jizya, they become dhimmah for us. Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, said: They only pay the jizya so that their blood becomes like our blood and their wealth like our wealth. And it will come in the book of sales the exception of their contracts on wine and pig, and that their contract on wine is like our contract on grape juice, and their contract on pig is like our contract on sheep. And we have presented that the dhimmi is held liable for hudud and qisas except for the hadd of drinking wine. And it was presented in the book of marriage that if they believe it is permissible without a dowry or witnesses or during the waiting period, they are left and what they believe, unlike riba, for it is excepted from their contracts. His statement: (And we do not fight those whom the call to Islam has not reached) meaning fighting is not permissible because of his saying, peace be upon him, in the instruction to commanders of armies: 'Call them to testify that there is no god but Allah,' and because through the call they know that we fight them for religion, not for seizing wealth and capturing slaves. From them, generally, unknown, as when he comes to a Muslim and says to him: 'Present Islam to me,' so he teaches him the two testimonies and he utters them willingly and by choice. And similarly, what has become customary in our time, that he goes to the court and accepts Islam before the judge, then there is no doubt and no uncertainty about his Islam. Even if he did not explicitly disavow, and deviation from what has come with clear evidence due to this mere probability is a rejection of the Shari'ah entirely. Indeed, Imam Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, only stipulated disavowal because of his certainty and knowledge of the condition of the people of his land and their belief that the prophethood is specific to other than the Children of Israel. Had he not known that from them, it would not have been permissible for him nor for those after him to contravene what the Shari'ah brought of being satisfied with the two testimonies. Thus, the ruling must be applied based on its cause in every time. Therefore, they said: It is not permissible for anyone to give fatwa according to our school until he knows from where we derived it. So seize this unique research. And what the author followed here, following Qari' al-Hidayah, al-'Ala'i mentioned in his commentary on al-Multaqa on apostasy that San'ullah Afandi gave fatwa in his fatawa, and that Ibn Kamal Pasha gave fatwa, and it was mentioned in the commentary on al-Multaqa by Damad Afandi that it is the practice. His statement: (They become dhimmah for us) al-Ramli said: It indicates that by mere acceptance they become dhimmah without a contract, and our call is sufficient beforehand. It also indicates that the imam is not permitted to refrain from taking them.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rules of jizya and the obligation of da'wah before fighting. It explains that if non-Muslims do not accept Islam, they are called to pay jizya, except for Arab polytheists and apostates who have no choice but Islam or death. When non-Muslims accept Islam and are in dar al-harb (non-Muslim territory), they are ordered to migrate to dar al-Islam, though this is not required if the land is contiguous. The imam must specify the jizya amounts. Once they accept jizya, they become dhimmis with rights and duties similar to Muslims in civil matters, except for certain prohibitions like alcohol and pork. The commentary then returns to the conversion issue: a person who comes to a Muslim and learns the shahada and utters it voluntarily becomes Muslim without needing to disavow his former religion, following the same principle that the requirement of tabarri was time-specific. The author stresses that fatwa must be based on understanding the underlying reasoning. The passage concludes with a quote from al-Ramli that dhimma is established by mere acceptance of jizya.

Key Terms
الجزية (al-jizya): A poll tax imposed on non-Muslims (dhimmis) in exchange for protection and exemption from military service.
الدعوة (al-da'wah): The call to Islam that must precede fighting according to the Hadith.
الذمة (al-dhimmah): The covenant of protection granted to non-Muslims who pay jizya, making them dhimmis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which group of non-Muslims is NOT eligible to pay jizya in the Hanafi school?
A) Christians
B) Jews
C) Zoroastrians
D) Arab polytheists

Key Takeaway
Da'wah must precede fighting, and jizya is only accepted from non-Muslims who are eligible per the Hanafi school, not from Arab polytheists or apostates.

Accepting Jizya and the Obligation of Da'wa Before Fighting
Ruling on jizya and prohibition of fighting without da'wa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on accepting jizya and the obligation of da'wa before fighting, and identify the legal consequences for dhimmis.

Core Idea
Fighting without prior call is haram; jizya acceptance instantly grants dhimma rights without a formal contract.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a foundational ruling in Islamic law of nations: the status of those who accept jizya and the absolute prohibition of fighting without first calling them to Islam. The Hanafi school derives this from the prophetic command to call before battle, and establishes that mere acceptance of jizya creates a covenant of protection (dhimma) with full rights and obligations. Understanding this ruling is essential for grasping the ethical framework of jihad and the legal status of non-Muslims in the Islamic state.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states two rulings: (1) If the enemy accepts jizya, they become dhimmis with rights and duties similar to Muslims. (2) Fighting is not permissible against those who have not received the call to Islam. Identify each ruling and its legal category.
The first ruling is a permission/establishment of status; the second is a prohibition.
2
Locate the dalil for the prohibition of fighting without da'wa
The author cites the Prophet's instruction to commanders: 'Call them to the testimony...' (hadith). Identify that this is a command (amr) which, in Hanafi usul, indicates obligation (wujub). Also note the rationale: so they know the fight is for religion, not for plunder.
The hadith is ahad, so its thubut is zanni, but its dalala is also zanni from the imperative. Hanafi usul treats amr as wajib.
3
Understand the distinction between real and constructive da'wa
The passage explains that the call can be literal (bi al-lisan) or constructive (by widespread knowledge). If the call has spread widely, it suffices. This distinction resolves apparent contradictions with historical surprise attacks.
This concept is unique to Hanafi jurisprudence and helps determine when fighting is permitted without a new individual call.
4
Analyze the consequences of accepting jizya
Once jizya is accepted, the people become dhimmis. Their blood and wealth are protected, and they are subject to hudud (except hadd for wine) and qisas. Their contracts involving wine and pork are treated analogously to Muslim contracts (e.g., wine contract like our contract on grape juice). The passage emphasizes that no formal contract is needed; mere acceptance suffices.
Al-Ramli's commentary clarifies that the Imam does not have the discretion to refuse their entry into dhimma after acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فإن قبلوا فلهم ما لنا وعليهم ما علينا أي قبلوا إعطاء الجزية صاروا ذمة لنا قال علي رضي الله عنه : إنما بذلوا الجزية لتكون دماؤهم كدمائنا وأموالهم كأموالنا. وسيأتي في البيوع استثناء عقدهم على الخمر والخنزير وأن عقدهم على الخمر كعقدنا على العصير، وعقدهم على الخنزير كعقدنا على الشاة، وقدمنا أن الذمي مؤاخذ بالحدود والقصاص إلا حد شرب الخمر، وتقدم في كتاب النكاح أنهم إذا اعتقدوا جوازه بغير مهر أو شهود أو في عدة تتركهم وما يدينون بخلاف الربا فإنه مستثنى من عقودهم
قوله : (ولا نقاتل من لا تبلغه الدعوة إلى الإسلام) أي لا يجوز القتال لقوله عليه السلام في وصية أمراء الأجناد: «فادعهم إلى شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله» ولأنهم بالدعوة يعلمون أنا نقاتلهم على الدين لا على سلب الأموال وسبي الذراري. أطلق الدعوة فشمل الحقيقية والحكمية فالحقيقية باللسان والحكمية انتشار الدعوة شرقاً وغرباً أنهم إلى ماذا يدعون وعلى ماذا يقاتلون فأقيم ظهورها مقامها، وقد نص محمد عليه في السير الكبير فقال : وإذا لقي المسلمون المشركين فإن كان المشركون قوماً لم يبلغهم الإسلام لا حقيقة ولا حكماً فلا ينبغي لهم أن يقاتلهم حتى يدعوهم إلى الإسلام. وفي فتح القدير : ولا شك أن في بلاد الله تعالى من لا شعور له بهذا الأمر فيجب أن المراد غلبة ظن أن هؤلاء لم تبلغهم الدعوة. وفي التتارخانية : وإن كانوا قوماً قد بلغهم الإسلام إلا أنهم لا يدرون أيقبل المسلمون الجزية أم لا فلا ينبغي لهم أن يقاتلهم حتى يدعوهم إلى الجزية اهـ قوله : (وصاروا ذمة لنا قال الرملي : يدل على أنه بمجرد القبول يصيرون ذمة من غير عقد ودعاؤنا قبل كافٍ، ويدل أيضاً على أن الإمام ليس له الامتناع من اتخاذهم
His saying: (For if they accept, then for them is what is for us and upon them is what is upon us) meaning they accept giving the jizya, they become a dhimma for us. Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) said: They only paid the jizya so that their blood becomes like our blood and their wealth like our wealth. And it will come in the Book of Sales the exception of their contracts on wine and pork, and that their contract on wine is like our contract on grape juice, and their contract on pork is like our contract on sheep. And we have presented that the dhimmi is held accountable by the hudud and qisas except the hadd for drinking wine. And it has preceded in the Book of Marriage that if they believe its permissibility without a dowry or witnesses or during waiting period, we leave them and their religion, contrary to riba for it is excepted from their contracts.
His saying: (And we do not fight those who the call to Islam has not reached) meaning fighting is not permissible because of his statement, peace be upon him, in the instruction to the commanders of armies: 'Call them to the testimony that there is no god but Allah.' And because by the call they know that we fight them for religion, not for plundering wealth and enslaving children. He made the call absolute, so it includes the real and the constructive: the real is by the tongue, and the constructive is the spread of the call east and west that they know to what they are called and for what they are fought, so its appearance stands in its place. And Muhammad (upon him mercy) stated in al-Siyar al-Kabir: When Muslims meet polytheists, if the polytheists are a people who have not been reached by Islam, whether real or constructive, it is not appropriate for them to fight them until they call them to Islam. And in Fath al-Qadir: No doubt that in Allah's lands there are those who have no awareness of this matter, so it must be that what is meant is a strong presumption that these have not been reached by the call. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If they are a people who have been reached by Islam but they do not know whether the Muslims accept jizya or not, it is not appropriate for them to fight them until they call them to jizya. End quote. His saying: (And they become a dhimma for us) al-Ramli said: It indicates that by mere acceptance they become a dhimma without a contract, and our call before is sufficient, and it also indicates that the Imam does not have the right to refrain from taking them.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings: first, when the enemy accepts jizya, they become dhimmis with rights and duties similar to Muslims (with exceptions like wine and pork contracts, and they are subject to hudud except for wine drinking). The second ruling is the prohibition of fighting those who have not received the call to Islam. The call can be literal (word of mouth) or constructive (widespread knowledge). Imam Muhammad in al-Siyar al-Kabir and Fath al-Qadir stress that fighting is not permissible until the call reaches them. The call to jizya is also needed if they don't know it's an option. The commentary by al-Ramli on 'they become a dhimma' shows that no formal contract is needed – acceptance itself suffices.

Key Terms
ذمة (dhimma): A covenant of protection granting non-Muslims security and certain rights in exchange for jizya.
دعوة حقيقية وحكمية (da'wah haqiqiyyah wa hukmiyyah): Literal call (by speech) and constructive call (widespread knowledge of Islam).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on fighting a group that has not been reached by the call to Islam?
A) It is permissible.
B) It is obligatory.
C) It is not permissible until they are called.
D) It is recommended.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that fighting without a prior call (real or constructive) is haram, and that accepting jizya instantly establishes a binding dhimma.

Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules

Permissible Battlefield Tactics and Their Conditions
Ruling with proof on permissible war tactics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using catapults, burning, cutting trees, and spoiling crops in war, and identify when such actions become disliked.

Core Idea
Destructive warfare tactics are permissible only when necessary; needless destruction is disliked.

Jihad, though a struggle, is governed by strict ethical rules. The Hanafi school permits certain harsh measures like catapults and burning enemy resources, but only when they serve a military necessity. This lesson explores the prophetic precedents for these actions and the crucial condition that limits them: if victory is already certain, such destruction becomes makruh (disliked). We also address the etiquette of raising voices in battle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their default ruling
The passage lists several battlefield actions: setting up catapults (majanīq), burning, drowning, cutting trees, spoiling crops, and shooting even when the enemy uses human shields. Identify the default ruling for each: they are mubah (permissible) based on prophetic practice.
The ruling is not absolute; it has conditions.
2
Analyze the prophetic evidence (dalil)
Each action is supported by the Prophet's ﷺ practice: catapults at Ta'if and burning the date-palm grove of Buwaira. Understand that these sunnah actions establish permissibility but not unlimited license.
The dalil is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala regarding unconditional permissibility.
3
Apply the necessity restriction
Fath al-Qadir restricts these actions to cases where victory is not already assured. If the enemy is clearly weakened and victory is imminent, destruction becomes makruh because it is corruption without need. Understand the shift from mubah to makruh based on the presence or absence of necessity.
This restriction is based on the principle of preventing unnecessary harm (al-ḍarar yuzāl).
4
Examine the etiquette of raising voices in battle
The passage distinguishes between raising the voice out of cowardice (fashal) – which is disliked – and raising it to encourage the Muslims – which is permissible. This shows that even non-destructive actions have ethical guidelines.
The hadith of the Companions applies to funerals, battle, and religious admonition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإلا فنستعين عليهم بالله تعالى بنصب المجانيق وحرقهم غرقهم وقطع أشجارهم وإفساد زروعهم ورميهم وإن تترسوا ببعضنا ونقصدهم) أي إن لم يقبلوا الجزية إلى آخره. أما الاستعانة فلأنه تعالى هو الناصر لأوليائه والمدمر على أعدائه فيستعان به في كل الأمور، وأما نصب المجانيق فلأنه عليه السلام نصبها على الطائف، وأما التحريق ونحوه فلأنه عليه السلام أحرق البوبرة أرسلوا عليهم الماء وقطعوا أشجارهم وأفسدوا زروعهم لأن في جميع ذلك إلحاق الغيظ والكبت بهم وكسر شوكتهم وتفريق جمعهم فيكون مشروعاً. أطلق في الأشجار فشمل المثمرة وغيرها كما في البدائع. وأطلق في جواز فعل هذه الأشياء وقيده في فتح القدير بما إذا لم يغلب على الظن أنهم مأخوذون بغير ذلك، فإن كان الظاهر أنهم مغلوبون وأن الفتح باد كره ذلك لأنه إفساد في غير محل الحاجة وما أبيح إلا لها وفي الظهيرية : ولا يستحب رفع الصوت في الحرب من غير أن يكون ذلك مكروهاً من وجه الدين ولكنه فشل والفشل الجبن، فإن كان فيه منفعة وتحريض للمسلمين فلا بأس به. وعن قيس بن عبادة قال : كان أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يكرهون الصوت عند ثلاث الجنائز والقتال والذكر. والمراد بالذكر الوعظ وقال الإمام شمس الأئمة السرخسي : ففي هذا الحديث بيان كراهة رفع الصوت عند سماع القرآن والوعظ فتبين به أن ما يفعله الذين يدعون الوجد والمحبة مكروه لا أصل له
His saying: (And otherwise we seek help from Allah Almighty by setting up catapults, burning them, drowning them, cutting their trees, spoiling their crops, shooting them, and if they use some of us as shields, we aim for them) meaning if they do not accept the jizya etc. As for seeking help, because He is the Helper of His allies and the Destroyer of His enemies, so He is sought for help in all matters. As for setting up catapults, because he (peace be upon him) set them up against al-Ta'if. As for burning and the like, because he (peace be upon him) burned the date-palm grove (al-Buwaira). They let water on them, cut their trees, and spoiled their crops because in all that is inflicting rage and suppression on them, breaking their power, and dispersing their assembly, so it is legitimate. He made absolute regarding trees, so it includes fruit-bearing and non-fruit-bearing as in al-Bada'i. And he made absolute the permissibility of these actions, but restricted in Fath al-Qadir to when it is not strongly presumed that they will be captured otherwise. For if it appears that they are overcome and victory is imminent, it is disliked because it is corruption in a place of non-necessity, and it was only permitted for necessity. And in al-Zahiriyya: It is not recommended to raise the voice in battle without it being disliked from a religious perspective, but it is cowardice (fashal). If there is benefit and incitement for the Muslims, then there is no harm. From Qays ibn 'Ubadah: The Companions of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) disliked raising the voice at three times: funerals, fighting, and dhikr. Dhikr means admonition. Imam Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: In this hadith is a clarification of the dislike of raising the voice when hearing the Quran and admonition, so it becomes clear that what those who claim ecstasy and love do is baseless and disliked.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage details the permissible methods of warfare when the enemy refuses jizya. The commentator justifies each method with prophetic practice: catapults used at Ta'if, burning the grove of Buwaira, cutting trees, and flooding. These are allowed as they weaken the enemy. However, Fath al-Qadir restricts them to cases of necessity; if victory is already certain, destructive acts are disliked (makruh) as they cause unnecessary harm. The passage also addresses raising voices in battle: it is disliked if it stems from cowardice, but permissible if it encourages the Muslims. A hadith from the Companions shows dislike of loud voices at funerals, battle, and admonition. Imam al-Sarakhsi uses this to criticize ecstatic practices in religious gatherings.

Key Terms
مجانيق (majanīq): Catapults or siege engines used to hurl projectiles at enemy fortifications.
فشل (fashal): Cowardice or weakness; raising voice in battle due to fashal is disliked.
بوارة (Buwaira): A date-palm grove belonging to the Banu al-Nadir; the Prophet ﷺ ordered it to be burned during the expedition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is it disliked (makruh) to cut trees and spoil crops in jihad?
A) When the enemy is a Muslim rebel group
B) When it is apparent that the enemy will be defeated without such actions
C) When the trees are fruit-bearing
D) It is never disliked; always permissible

Key Takeaway
Battlefield destruction is permitted but conditioned on necessity; avoid unnecessary harm.

The Ruling on Invitation (Da'wah) Before Jihad: Wajib or Recommended?
Explaining the obligation and recommendation of inviting enemies to Islam before fighting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inviting the enemy to Islam before fighting, distinguish between actual and legal da'wah, and apply the conditions that modify the obligation to the recommendation.

Core Idea
Invitation is wajib only for those unreached by the call; for those already informed, it is recommended but not required.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a critical question in the fiqh of jihad: does a Muslim army have to invite the enemy to Islam before engaging in battle? The Hanafi school, drawing on the Siyar of Imam Muhammad, distinguishes between two categories of da'wah—actual and legal—and clarifies that the ruling depends on whether the enemy has already received the message. The author also discusses an exception: when the invitation would cause harm by alerting the enemy, the recommendation is dropped. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of da'wah
Read the text carefully to find where the author distinguishes between actual (haqiqiyyah) and legal (hukmiyyah) da'wah. Understand that actual is verbal, legal is constructive notification through widespread propagation.
The text says: 'أطلق الدعوة فشمل الحقيقية والحكمية فالحقيقية باللسان والحكمية انتشار الدعوة شرقاً وغرباً.'
2
Distinguish the ruling: wajib vs. nadb
Identify that the ruling varies based on whether the enemy has been reached by the call. For those unreached, it is wajib; for those reached, it is nadb (recommended) but not wajib.
Imam Muhammad's statement: 'لا ينبغي لهم أن يقاتلوهم حتى يدعوهم إلى الإسلام' indicates obligation for the unreached.
3
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith evidence cited: the Prophet's raid on Banu al-Mustaliq without prior da'wah and his order to Usama to raid Ubna by surprise. This shows that da'wah is not obligatory in every case.
The text: 'لأنه صح أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أغار على بني المصطلق وهم غارون...'
4
Apply the condition of harm
Note that the recommendation (nadb) is qualified: if the invitation would cause harm (e.g., giving the enemy time to prepare), it is not recommended. The text states this restriction from Fath al-Qadir.
The text: 'أطلق في الاستحباب وهو مقيد بأن لا يتضمن ضرراً...'
5
Note cross-madhhab context
Understand that the Hanafi position is not unanimous across schools; some consider da'wah obligatory in all cases. Recognize the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
From hanafi_position_box: cross_madhhab_status is mukhtalaf_fih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السير
فنستعين عليهم بالله تعالى ونحاربهم بنصب المجانيق وحرقهم وغرقهم وقطع أشجارهم وإفساد زروعهم ورميهم وإن تترسوا ببعضنا ونقصدهم ونهينا عن إخراج مصحف الذراري فلعلهم يجيبون فنكفي مؤنة القتال ولو قاتلهم قبل الدعوة أثم للنهي ولا غرامة لعدم العاصم وهو الدين أو الإحراز بالدار فصار كقتل النسوان والصبيان. أطلق الدعوة فشمل الحقيقية والحكمية فالحقيقية باللسان والحكمية انتشار الدعوة شرقاً وغرباً أنهم إلى ماذا يدعون وعلى ماذا يقاتلون فأقيم ظهورها مقامها، وقد نص محمد عليه في السير الكبير فقال : وإذا لقي المسلمون المشركين فإن كان المشركون قوماً لم يبلغهم الإسلام لا حقيقة ولا حكماً فلا ينبغي لهم أن يقاتلوهم حتى يدعوهم إلى الإسلام، وفي فتح القدير: ولا شك أن في بلاد الله تعالى من لا شعور له بهذا الأمر فيجب أن المراد غلبة ظن أن هؤلاء لم تبلغهم الدعوة. وفي التتارخانية : وإن كانوا قوماً قد بلغهم الإسلام إلا أنهم لا يدرون أيقبل المسلمون الجزية أم لا فلا ينبغي لهم أن يقاتلوهم حتى يدعوهم إلى الجزية اهـ قوله : (وندعو ندباً من بلغته أي الدعوة مبالغة في الإنذار ولا يجب ذلك لأنه صح أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أغار على بني المصطلق وهم غارون وعهد إلى أسامة أن يغير على أبنى صباحاً ثم يحرق، والغارة لا تكون بدعوة. وأبنى بوزن حبلى موضع بالشام أطلق في الاستحباب وهو مقيد بأن لا يتضمن ضرراً بأن يعلم أنهم بالدعوة يستعدون أو يحتالون أو يتحصنون وغلبة الظن في ذلك بما يظهر من أحوالهم كالعلم؛ كذا في فتح القدير .
Book of Siyar. So we seek help from Allah against them and fight them by erecting mangonels, burning them, drowning them, cutting their trees, spoiling their crops, and shooting at them even if they shield themselves with some of us, and we aim at them. And we are prohibited from taking out a copy of the Qur'an or women into enemy territory? Perhaps they will respond and we will be spared the trouble of fighting. If one fights them before the invitation, he sins due to the prohibition, and there is no compensation because there is no protectiveness which is the religion or secure residence in the abode of war. So it becomes like killing women and children. He made the invitation absolute, so it includes the actual and legal: the actual is oral, the legal is the spread of the invitation east and west, that they invite to what and fight over what, so its emergence is established in its place. And Muhammad stated in al-Siyar al-Kabir: 'When Muslims meet polytheists, if the polytheists are a people who have not received Islam, neither actually nor legally, then it is not proper for them to fight them until they invite them to Islam.' In Fath al-Qadir: 'There is no doubt that in the lands of Allah there are those who have no awareness of this matter, so what is meant is that the dominant assumption is that these people have not been reached by the invitation.' In al-Tatarkhaniyya: 'If they are a people who have been reached by Islam but they do not know whether the Muslims will accept jizya or not, then it is not proper for them to fight them until they invite them to jizya.' End. He said: 'We invite as a recommendation one whom the invitation has reached as an exaggeration in warning, and it is not obligatory because it is authentically reported that the Prophet, peace be upon him, raided Banu al-Mustaliq while they were heedless, and he entrusted Usama to raid Ubna in the morning and then burn, and a raid is not preceded by an invitation. And Ubna, on the pattern of Hubna, is a place in Syria. He made the recommendation absolute, but it is restricted by not involving harm, such as knowing that by the invitation they will prepare or resort to tricks or fortify themselves, and dominant assumption in that based on what appears of their situation is like knowledge. Thus in Fath al-Qadir.'
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with the ruling on inviting the enemy to Islam before engaging in combat. The author opens by stating that fighting involves various strategies, and then clarifies that if one fights without having issued a da'wah (invitation), he is sinful. The reason is that the enemy, when invited, may accept Islam or agree to jizya, thus avoiding bloodshed. The text distinguishes between actual da'wah (verbal invitation) and legal da'wah (when the message of Islam has spread widely such that the enemy is considered to have been notified constructively). Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani in al-Siyar al-Kabir explicitly states that if the enemy has not received the call to Islam—neither actually nor constructively—fighting them is not permissible until they are invited. Additional references from Fath al-Qadir and al-Tatarkhaniyya elaborate that the ruling is based on the dominant assumption about whether the call has reached them. The text then explains that the recommended (nadb) invitation for those who have already been reached by the call is not obligatory. The proof is that the Prophet himself raided Banu al-Mustaliq without a prior invitation, and he ordered Usama to raid Ubna by surprise. This indicates that da'wah is not always required before fighting. However, the recommendation is qualified: if inviting would cause harm, such as giving the enemy time to prepare defenses, then it is not recommended. This ruling is based on the principle that Jihad is a collective obligation aimed at the supremacy of Islam, and the da'wah is a means to that end.

Key Terms
الدعوة الحقيقية (al-da'wah al-haqiqiyyah): The actual verbal invitation to Islam given by Muslims to the enemy before battle.
الدعوة الحكمية (al-da'wah al-hukmiyyah): The constructive notification that occurs when the message of Islam has spread widely so that the enemy is deemed to have been informed.
ندب (nadb): A recommended act; here, inviting those who have already heard the call is recommended, not obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is it wajib to invite the enemy to Islam before fighting?
A) In all cases, regardless of whether the enemy has heard of Islam.
B) Only if the enemy has clearly not received the call to Islam, actually or constructively.
C) Only if the enemy is from the People of the Book.
D) Never; da'wah is always recommended but never wajib.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that inviting the enemy to Islam before battle is obligatory only for those who have not received the call; for those who have, it is recommended but not required, and the recommendation is dropped if it would cause harm.

Permissible War Tactics and the Human Shield Ruling
Details permissibility of war tactics and human shield ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using mangonels, burning, and destroying enemy property in jihad, and apply the ruling on human shields (tatarruṣ) to determine permissibility and compensation.

Core Idea
Jihad permits accidental Muslim casualties, but unnecessary destruction is makruh.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that while jihad permits powerful means like mangonels and burning, these are not unrestricted. The Hanafi school balances collective obligation with individual harm, especially in the case of human shields, where the greater good justifies the risk. Understanding the conditions and exceptions is crucial for correct application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their default ruling
List the war tactics mentioned: mangonels, burning, drowning, cutting trees, spoiling crops, and shooting. The default ruling is mubah (permissible) because they are based on the Prophet's actions.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Prophet used a mangonel at Ta'if and burned the palm trees of Bani al-Nadir. These actions establish the permissibility of such means.
3
Note the condition for permissibility
The permissibility is restricted to cases of genuine need. If victory is imminent without destruction, such actions become makruh because they amount to unnecessary corruption.
This condition is from Fath al-Qadir.
4
Analyze the human shield ruling
If the enemy shields themselves with Muslims (prisoners, merchants, children), it is permissible to shoot with the intention to hit the enemy. The general harm to Islam outweighs the specific harm to the Muslim shield.
5
Determine the consequences
No blood money (diyah) or expiation (kaffara) is owed for accidental Muslim deaths in this context because jihad is a collective obligation that overrides financial penalties.
Contrast with the case of dire necessity (makhmasa) where liability is considered.

Source Text

ويندب للمجاهد في دار الحرب توفير الأظفار وإن كان قصها من الفطرة لأنه إذا سقط السلاح من يده ودنا منه العدو ربما يتمكن من دفعه بأظافيره وهو نظير قص الشوارب فإنه سنة، ثم الغازي في دار الحرب مندوب إلى توفيرها وتطويلها ليكون أهيب في عين من يبارزه. والحاصل أن ما يعين المرء على الجهاد فهو مندوب إلى اكتسابه لما فيه من إعزاز المسلمين وقهر المشركين اهـ. وأما جواز رميهم وإن تترسوا ببعضنا فلأن في الرمي دفع الضرر العام بالذب عن بيضة الإسلام وقتل المسلم ضرر خاص، ولأنه قل ما يحلو حصن عن مسلم فلو امتنع عن اعتباره لا نسد بابه أطلق في بعضنا فشمل الأسير والتاجر والصبيان لكن نقصد الكفار بالرمي دون المسلمين لأنه إن تعذر التمييز فعلاً فقد أمكن قصداً والطاعة بحسب الطاقة، وما أصابوه منهم لا دية عليهم ولا كفارة لأن الجهاد فرض والغرامات لا تقترن بالفروض بخلاف حالة المخمصة لأنه لا يمتنع مخافة الضمان لما فيه من إحياء نفسه، أما الجهاد بني على إتلاف النفس فيمتنع حذار الضمان، وأما قوله عليه السلام ليس في الإسلام دم «مفرج أي مهدر فمعناه ليس في دار الإسلام وكلامنا في دار الحرب؛ كذا في العناية قيد بالتترس عند المحاربة لأن الإمام إذا فتح بلدة ومعلوم أن فيها مسلماً أو ذمياً لا يحل قتل أحد منهم لاحتمال كونه ذلك المسلم أو الذمي ولو أخرج واحداً من عرض الناس حل إذا قتل الباقي الجواز كون المخرج هو ذلك فصار في كون المسلم في الباقين شك بخلاف الحالة الأولى فإن كون المسلم أو الذمي فيهم معلوم بالفرض فوقع الفرق؛ كذا في فتح القدير.
He said: 'Otherwise we seek help from Allah against them by erecting mangonels, burning them, drowning them, cutting their trees, spoiling their crops, and shooting at them even if they shield themselves with some of us, and we aim at them.' That is if they do not accept jizya etc. As for seeking help, because He is the Helper of His allies and the Destroyer of His enemies, so He is sought for help in all matters. As for erecting mangonels, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, erected them against Ta'if. As for burning and the like, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, burned the palm trees? They sent water upon them, cut their trees, and spoiled their crops because in all that is inflicting rage and suppression upon them, breaking their power, and dispersing their assembly, so it is legislated. He made absolute in trees to include fruit-bearing and non-fruit-bearing as in al-Bada'i'. And he made absolute the permissibility of these actions, but it is restricted in Fath al-Qadir to when it is not the dominant assumption that they will be taken otherwise. If it appears that they are overwhelmed and victory is imminent, it is disliked because it is corruption without need, and it was only permitted for need. And in al-Zahiriyya: 'It is not recommended to raise the voice in war, though it is not disliked from the religious perspective, but it is cowardice. If there is benefit and encouragement for the Muslims, then there is no harm.' And from Qays ibn 'Ubada: 'The Companions of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, disliked raising the voice at three occasions: funerals, fighting, and dhikr.' And dhikr means preaching. Imam Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: 'In this hadith is a statement of the dislike of raising the voice when hearing the Qur'an and preaching, so it becomes clear that what those who claim ecstasy and love do is disliked and has no basis in religion. And it becomes clear that it prevents the ascetics and fools among the Sufis from their habit of raising the voice and tearing garments at hearings, because that is disliked in religion when hearing the Qur'an and preaching. What do you think of hearing singing?' And it is recommended for the mujahid in the abode of war to grow his nails, even though cutting them is from the fitra, because if the weapon falls from his hand and the enemy approaches, perhaps he can defend himself with his nails. It is analogous to cutting the mustache which is sunna. Then the warrior in the abode of war is recommended to grow them and lengthen them to be more awe-inspiring in the eye of his opponent. The conclusion is that whatever helps a person in jihad is recommended for him to acquire because of the honor of Muslims and the humbling of polytheists. End. As for the permissibility of shooting at them even if they shield themselves with some of us, it is because in shooting there is repelling general harm by defending the citadel of Islam, and killing a Muslim is specific harm. And because rarely does a fortress remain without a Muslim, so if one refrains from considering that, the door would be blocked. He made 'some of us' absolute to include the prisoner, the merchant, and children, but we aim at the disbelievers in shooting, not at the Muslims, because if actual distinction is impossible, intentional distinction is possible, and obedience is according to ability. Whatever hits them of Muslims, there is no blood-money nor expiation for them because jihad is a collective obligation and penalties are not attached to obligations, unlike the case of dire necessity because one does not refrain due to fear of liability in order to save himself. Whereas jihad is built on self-destruction, so it refrains due to fear of liability. And as for his statement, peace be upon him, 'There is no wasted? blood in Islam', its meaning is in the abode of Islam, and our discussion is in the abode of war. Thus in al-'Inaya. It is restricted by the tatarruṣ at the time of battle because when the Imam conquers a town and it is known that there is a Muslim or dhimmi in it, it is not permissible to kill any of them due to the possibility that he is that Muslim or dhimmi. But if one is brought out from the general populace, it is permissible to kill the rest? The permissibility of killing the rest is because the one brought out is that person, so there is doubt about the remaining Muslims. Contrary to the first case, where the existence of a Muslim or dhimmi among them is known by assumption, so a distinction is made. Thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This section of al-Bahr al-Raiq elaborates on the permissible means of warfare in the Hanafi school. After stating that if the enemy refuses jizya, Muslims may use various methods, the author provides proofs: the use of mangonels is established by the Prophet's siege of Ta'if; burning and destroying trees and crops is based on the Prophet's actions against Bani al-Nadir (the 'burning of the palm trees'). However, the author qualifies that destructive actions are only permitted when necessary for victory; if victory is already imminent without them, they become disliked because they constitute unnecessary corruption. The text then addresses the issue of raising voices in battle: it is not recommended but not prohibited unless it serves a beneficial purpose like encouraging Muslims. A hadith is cited showing that the Companions disliked raising voices at funerals, battle, and dhikr (preaching). Imam al-Sarakhsi uses this to criticize certain Sufi practices of loud vocalizations and tearing clothes at religious gatherings, extending the dislike to such practices even at hearing the Qur'an, and even more so at hearing singing. The text then turns to the recommendation for a warrior to grow his nails for self-defense during hand-to-hand combat, though cutting nails is normally sunna. The main ruling on human shields (tatarruṣ) is then presented: if the enemy uses Muslims as shields, it is permissible to shoot at them with the intention of hitting the enemy, and no blood money or expiation is owed if a Muslim is unintentionally killed. This is because jihad is a fard kifayah that overrides the specific prohibition against killing Muslims, and because the aim is to repel the general threat to Islam. The text also distinguishes between the case of a besieged fortress where Muslims are definitely present (in which case it is not permissible to indiscriminately kill them) and the case of a town where the presence of a Muslim is probable but not certain (where killing is permissible). The last part mentions that if the caliph opens a town known to contain Muslims, it is not allowed to kill anyone, but doubt allows the killing of the rest as per some opinions.

Key Terms
تترس (tatarruṣ): The act of enemies using Muslim prisoners or civilians as human shields to protect themselves from attack.
أظفار (azfar): Fingernails; recommended for a warrior in Dar al-Harb to grow long for self-defense.
مصاحف (masāhif): Copies of the Qur'an; prohibited from being taken into enemy territory in a raiding party due to risk of desecration.
سرية (sariyya): A military detachment sent on a raid without the full army.
مخمصة (makhmasa): Extreme hunger or famine; a case of necessity that permits eating otherwise forbidden food.
غرامة (gharama): Financial compensation or penalty, such as diyah (blood money) or kaffara (expiation).
فشل (fashal): Cowardice or weakness; raising voice in battle may indicate cowardice unless it boosts morale.
هيبة (hayba): Awe; growing nails and hair in battle is recommended to inspire awe in the enemy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the enemy uses Muslim prisoners as human shields, what is the ruling on shooting at them?
A) It is absolutely prohibited because killing a Muslim is haram.
B) It is permissible, and no blood money is due if a Muslim is accidentally killed.
C) It is permissible but blood money must be paid for any Muslim killed.
D) It is only permissible if the Muslim shield gives explicit permission.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits strong war tactics and accidental Muslim casualties in jihad, but conditions them on genuine necessity, and no financial compensation is owed for such accidents.

The Prohibition of Taking Women and Qur'an into Enemy Territory
Ruling statement with qualification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on taking women and copies of the Qur'an into a sariyya and identify the condition that renders it prohibited or permissible.

Core Idea
Taking women and Qur'an into enemy territory is prohibited when there is genuine fear of capture; otherwise it may be permissible.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a specific ruling in jihad: the prohibition of taking women and copies of the Qur'an into a raiding party (sariyya). The ruling is not absolute; it is tied to the risk of capture. The Hanafi school emphasizes the harm that could result—capture of women and desecration of the Qur'an—and qualifies the prohibition accordingly. Understanding this qualification helps us see how fiqh balances practical risk with general principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is taking women and copies of the Qur'an into a sariyya (raiding party sent into enemy territory). The default ruling from the text is prohibition (nahy).
The text says 'we were prohibited' (nuhina).
2
Identify the qualifying condition
The prohibition is conditional: 'when there is fear for them' (idha khifa alayha). If there is no fear (e.g., the raid is safe), the ruling may change.
The phrase 'yukhaafu alayha' indicates the condition.
3
Understand the illa (reason) behind the prohibition
The reason (illa) is 'exposing them to risk of being taken captive' (ta'riduhunna ala al-daman). This includes both women and the Qur'an.
Daman here means risk of capture.
4
Apply the condition: when is the ruling different?
If there is no fear (e.g., the sariyya is strong and the enemy weak), the prohibition may be lifted, and it could be permissible or even mustahabb to take the Qur'an for recitation or women for support.
The author says 'it must be restricted to when there is no fear of a bad outcome' (in lam yukhof su' 'aqibatin).
5
Note the caveat: 'ta'ammul' indicates need for ijtihad
The passage ends with 'ponder' (ta'ammul), suggesting that this qualification requires careful consideration of circumstances and ijtihad by the scholar or commander.
Ta'ammul is a call to reflect, not a mechanical rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ونهينا عن إخراج مصحف وامرأة في سرية يخاف عليها) لأن فيه تعريضهن على الضمان فلم يكن ذمة ويجب تقييده بما إذا لم يخف سوء عاقبة منه تأمل
He said: 'And we were prohibited from taking out a copy of the Qur'an and a woman in a raiding party when there is fear for them' because in that there is exposing them to risk of being taken captive, so there is no protection, and it must be restricted to when there is no fear of a bad outcome from it. Ponder.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This brief passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq states a prohibition: when Muslims send a raiding party (sariyya) into enemy territory, they should not take with them copies of the Qur'an or women. The reason given is that if the enemy captures them, the women may be taken captive and the Qur'an may be desecrated. The ruling is qualified: the prohibition applies when there is fear of such consequences; if there is no fear (e.g., the raid is likely to succeed and the women are safe), it may not be prohibited. The author says 'ponder' (ta'ammul), indicating that this qualification requires careful consideration.

Key Terms
سرية (sariyya): A military detachment sent on a raid, smaller than a full army.
مصحف (mushaf): A bound copy of the Qur'an.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee; here referring to the risk of women being taken as captives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because women are not allowed in war.
B) Because of the risk of them falling into enemy hands and being captured or desecrated.
C) Because it distracts the warriors.
D) Because it is forbidden to take the Qur'an out of the Muslim territory.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on taking women and the Qur'an into a sariyya is conditional on the presence of fear of capture; when fear is absent, the prohibition is lifted.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Jumping from a Burning Ship
Intra-madhhab difference on self-preservation versus self-destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on jumping into water from a burning ship, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of this dispute.

Core Idea
When fire is certain, jumping is permissible according to Abu Hanifa, but Muhammad prohibits it unless the fire has touched.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding Muslims on a ship that catches fire. The question: can they jump into the water to escape the flames? The answer hinges on whether the jump constitutes self-destruction (ihlak) or a permissible choice of the lesser harm. We'll explore the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf versus their student Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the two options
Identify the factual scenario: a ship on fire, two options: stay and burn or jump into the water. Both options likely lead to death.
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: permissible to jump. Muhammad al-Shaybani: not permissible unless the fire has touched their bodies.
Muhammad's position is more restrictive.
3
Examine the proofs
For Abu Hanifa, the principle of choosing the lesser harm; for Muhammad, the prohibition of direct self-harm (ihlak bi-fi'l).
The hadith 'No harm and no reciprocating harm' is invoked.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, jumping is mubah and no sin; under Muhammad, jumping before fire touches is sinful.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon view in the school is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, permitting jumping.
This follows the principle of necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تأمل قوله : (بخلاف حالة المخمصة) قال في الفتح واعلم أن المذهب عندنا في المضطر أنه لا يجب عليه أكل مال الغير مع فرضاً فهو كالمباح يتقيد بشرط السلامة كالمرور في الطريق فلا حاجة إلى الفرق بينه وبين افتراض الجهاد في نفي الضمان .اهـ. قوله : (وقال محمد لا يجوز لهم أن يلقوا أنفسهم في الماء) قال في التتارخانية : هذا إذا لم تصب النار بدنهم، أما إذا أصابت فإنهم يلقون أنفسهم في الماء لأن فيه أدنى [؟]
Ponder his statement: 'Unlike the case of dire necessity.' He said in al-Fath: Know that our school regarding the one compelled is that it is not obligatory for him to eat the property of another, even if it is fard? It is like permissible things, conditioned by safety like passing on the road. So there is no need to distinguish between it and the obligation of jihad in negating liability. End. He said: 'And Muhammad said: It is not permissible for them to throw themselves into the water.' He said in al-Tatarkhaniyya: This is when the fire has not touched their bodies; if it touches, then they throw themselves into the water because it is the lesser [of two harms?].
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage deals with two related issues. First, the author comments on the distinction between jihad and cases of dire necessity (makhmasa). In al-Fath, it is clarified that in the Hanafi school, a person compelled by extreme hunger is not obliged to eat another's property even if it is a necessity; it is treated like a permissible act conditioned by safety. Therefore, there is no need to distinguish between jihad and necessity regarding the negation of liability—in both cases, liability is waived. Then the text moves to a specific case: if Muslims are on a ship that catches fire, and they have two choices: stay and burn or jump into water and possibly drown. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf give them the choice because both outcomes are equally fatal. However, Muhammad al-Shaybani says they may not jump because that would be direct self-destruction, unless the fire has actually touched their bodies, in which case jumping is permissible as the lesser harm. This is a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf based on the principle of preferring the lesser evil. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) view is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as jumping is not considered self-destruction when it is the only way to avoid certain death by fire.

Key Terms
مضطر (mudtar): A person in dire necessity, such as extreme hunger, who may be permitted to do otherwise forbidden acts.
إهلاك (ihlak): Self-destruction; Muhammad's view is that jumping into water is a form of self-destruction.
غرامة (gharama): Liability; negated in jihad and in necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, when is it permissible for Muslims on a burning ship to jump into the water?
A) Always permissible.
B) Never permissible.
C) Permissible only if the fire has actually touched their bodies.
D) Permissible only if they can swim.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, when death is inevitable, jumping into water to avoid fire is permissible according to the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), and this is the relied-upon view.

Permissibility of Shooting When Enemy Uses Muslim Shields
A ruling on the permissibility of targeting enemies even when they shield themselves with Muslims, with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on targeting enemies who use Muslim shields, explain the rationale of repelling general harm over specific harm, and identify the conditions under which no diya or kaffarah is due.

Core Idea
Defending the Muslim community justifies collateral harm to individual Muslim shields in active battle.

In the heat of battle, enemies may use Muslim prisoners or civilians as human shields. Is it permissible for Muslim fighters to shoot, knowing a Muslim may be hit? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling based on the principle of repelling general harm (ḍarar 'āmm) over specific harm (ḍarar khāṣṣ), drawing from the works of al-‛Ināyah and Fatḥ al-Qadīr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: shooting at enemies who use Muslim shields. The Hanafi ruling is that it is permissible (jā'iz), and in context it becomes a collective obligation (fard kifāyah) due to defending Islam.
The permissibility is contingent on the intention to target disbelievers.
2
Analyze the first proof: general vs specific harm
Understand the rationale: shooting repels general harm (the threat to the entire Islamic community) while causing specific harm (potential death of a Muslim). The principle is that repelling general harm takes precedence over specific harm.
This is a foundational qā'idah in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyze the second proof: practical necessity
Note that fortresses rarely exist without a Muslim inside. If we forbade shooting in every such case, the door to jihad would be closed. Thus, necessity permits the action.
This is a practical consideration for the implementation of jihad.
4
Address the claim about diya and kaffarah
If a Muslim is unintentionally killed, no blood money (diya) or expiation (kaffarah) is due. The reason: jihad is an obligation, and penalties are not attached to obligations. This contrasts with the case of dire hunger where one eats forbidden food – there, the act is to save life, not to destroy it.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the legal consequences.
5
Interpret the hadith 'There is no wasted blood in Islam'
The hadith is interpreted by Hanafis to apply only within Dār al-Islām. Our case is in Dār al-Ḥarb, so the hadith does not prohibit the action.
This shows the importance of contextual interpretation (tafsīr) of Prophetic statements.
6
Distinguish from the case of a conquered town
If a town is conquered and it is known that Muslims or dhimmīs are present, no one may be killed indiscriminately. But if a specific person is brought out, the rest become permissible. In the battle scenario with shields, the presence of Muslims is known by assumption, but intention allows it.
This nuance prevents misuse of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما جواز رميهم وإن تترسوا ببعضنا فلأن في الرمي دفع الضرر العام بالذب عن بيضة الإسلام وقتل المسلم ضرر خاص، ولأنه قل ما يحلو حصن عن مسلم فلو امتنع عن اعتباره لا نسد بابه أطلق في بعضنا فشمل الأسير والتاجر والصبيان لكن نقصد الكفار بالرمي دون المسلمين لأنه إن تعذر التمييز فعلاً فقد أمكن قصداً والطاعة بحسب الطاقة، وما أصابوه منهم لا دية عليهم ولا كفارة لأن الجهاد فرض والغرامات لا تقترن بالفروض بخلاف حالة المخمصة لأنه لا يمتنع مخافة الضمان لما فيه من إحياء نفسه، أما الجهاد بني على إتلاف النفس فيمتنع حذار الضمان، وأما قوله عليه السلام ليس في الإسلام دم مفرج أي مهدر فمعناه ليس في دار الإسلام وكلامنا في دار الحرب؛ كذا في العناية قيد بالتترس عند المحاربة لأن الإمام إذا فتح بلدة ومعلوم أن فيها مسلماً أو ذمياً لا يحل قتل أحد منهم لاحتمال كونه ذلك المسلم أو الذمي ولو أخرج واحداً من عرض الناس حل إذا قتل الباقي الجواز كون المخرج هو ذلك فصار في كون المسلم في الباقين شك بخلاف الحالة الأولى فإن كون المسلم أو الذمي فيهم معلوم بالفرض فوقع الفرق؛ كذا في فتح القدير.
As for the permissibility of shooting them even if they shield themselves with some of us, it is because shooting repels general harm by defending the core of Islam, while killing the Muslim is specific harm. And because rarely does a fortress remain without a Muslim; if one refrains from considering that, the door would be closed. The phrase 'some of us' is general, including the captive, the merchant, and children, but we intend the disbelievers with the shooting, not the Muslims, because even if actual distinction is impossible, intentional distinction is possible, and obedience is according to capacity. Whatever they [the Muslims] are hit from them, there is no blood money and no expiation, because jihad is an obligation, and penalties are not attached to obligations—unlike the state of dire hunger, where one does not refrain out of fear of liability because it involves saving one's own life. As for jihad, it is based on the destruction of the self, so one refrains out of fear of liability. As for his statement, peace be upon him, 'There is no wasted blood in Islam'—meaning no blood is without liability—its meaning is in the abode of Islam, and our discussion is in the abode of war. Thus in Al-‛Ināyah, it is restricted to tatarruq during battle, because if the imam conquers a town and it is known that there is a Muslim or a dhimmī in it, it is not permissible to kill any of them due to the possibility that the victim is that Muslim or dhimmī. But if one specific person is brought out from the general populace, it becomes permissible to kill the rest because the one brought out is known, so there is doubt about the Muslim being among the rest—unlike the first case where the presence of the Muslim or dhimmī is known by assumption. Thus the difference is established; as in Fatḥ al-Qadīr.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling and its proofs for shooting at enemies who use Muslims as human shields (tatarruq) during an active battle. The author first gives the justification: the general benefit of defending the Muslim community outweighs the specific harm to individual Muslims who are shields. Secondly, it is rare for a fortress to contain no Muslim; forbidding shooting would make jihad impracticable. The word 'some of us' includes captives, merchants, and children, but the intention must be to target the disbelievers. Since actual distinction may be difficult, the intention suffices. No diya or kaffarah is due if Muslims are unintentionally killed because jihad is an obligation and such penalties are not attached to obligations. This contrasts with the case of dire hunger where one may eat forbidden food without liability; the reasoning differs because in jihad the act is based on destroying life while in hunger it is about saving life. The hadith about 'no wasted blood in Islam' is then addressed: it applies only in Dar al-Islam, not Dar al-Harb. Finally, a distinction is made between a conquered town where the presence of Muslims/dhimmis is known (forbidding indiscriminate shooting) versus a battle where shields are used (permitting shooting with caution).

Key Terms
التترس (al-tatarruq): The act of using others as shields, specifically here when enemies use Muslims as shields during warfare.
بيضة الإسلام (bayḍat al-Islām): The core or heartland of the Islamic community, that which must be defended.
ضرر عام وضرر خاص (ḍarar 'āmm wa ḍarar khāṣṣ): General harm vs. specific harm: the general harm (to the community) is repelled even if it causes specific harm (to individual Muslims).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is no blood money (diya) or expiation (kaffarah) due if a Muslim is inadvertently killed while shooting at enemies using Muslim shields?
A) Because the Muslim was a traitor
B) Because jihad is an obligation and penalties are not attached to obligations
C) Because the shooter was acting in self-defense
D) Because the victim's family consented

Key Takeaway
Shooting at enemies using Muslim shields is permissible in the Hanafi school because repelling general harm (defence of Islam) outweighs specific harm (losing a Muslim), and no monetary penalty is incurred due to the obligatory nature of jihad.

Muslims on a Burning Ship: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Equal Harm
Intra-Hanafi ruling with ta'līl on equal harms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on whether Muslims on a burning ship may jump into the sea when staying and jumping equally lead to certain death, and apply the ruling to analogous cases of unavoidable harm.

Core Idea
When staying and jumping are equally deadly, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf permit the choice; Muhammad prohibits it as self-killing.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute over a desperate scenario: Muslims on a burning ship with no hope of escape. The key question is whether actively causing one's own death (by jumping) is permissible when the alternative is certain death by fire. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that the victims have a choice (khiyār), while Muhammad al-Shaybani prohibits jumping because it constitutes self-destruction. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify how different principles (prevention of harm vs. prohibition of self-killing) interact in extreme cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios distinguished in the passage
The passage first describes a case where jumping gives a real chance of survival (obligation to jump). Then it presents the contested case where both outcomes are certain death. Recognize that the dispute only arises in the second scenario.
The first scenario (ghalabat al-ẓann of salvation) is uncontroversial and establishes the baseline.
2
State the positions of the two parties
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: khiyār (choice) – the victims may stay or jump. Muhammad: lā yajūz (not permissible) – they may not jump; they must stay and burn.
3
Identify the reasoning (ta'līl) behind each position
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf reason that since both outcomes are equally bad, neither act is preferable, so the choice is neutral (mubah). Muhammad argues that jumping is an active act that directly causes death, thus it is self-killing (haram), whereas staying is passive and the fire is an external cause.
The key distinction is between active vs. passive causation of death.
4
Determine the muftā bihī (relied-upon) position
The text indicates that the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is the established zahir al-riwāyah and is acted upon. Thus the muftā bihī is the permissibility of choice.
Later Hanafi works generally follow this majority view.
5
Apply the ruling to a parallel scenario
Consider a Muslim trapped in a burning building with a fatal fall as the only alternative. According to the muftā bihī, he may jump. According to Muhammad, he must stay. Practically, most modern Hanafi fatwas would permit the choice.
This illustrates that even in extreme duress, the school does not automatically permit any act of self-destruction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الولوالجية وغيرها فإن كان المسلمون في سفينة فاحترقت السفينة، فإن كان غلبة ظنهم أنهم لو ألقوا أنفسهم في البحر تخلصوا بالسباحة يجب عليهم أن يطرحوا أنفسهم في البحر ليتخلصوا من الهلاك القطعي، وإن استوى الجانبان إن أقاموا احترقوا وإن أوقعوا أنفسهم غرقوا فهم بالخيار عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف لاستواء الجانبين. وقال محمد: لا يجوز لهم أن يلقوا أنفسهم في الماء لأنه يكون إهلاكاً بفعلهم ا هـ.
And in Al-Walwālijiyyah and others: If Muslims are on a ship and the ship catches fire, and if their preponderant belief is that if they throw themselves into the sea they will be saved by swimming, they must throw themselves into the sea to escape certain destruction. But if both sides are equal—if they stay they burn, and if they throw themselves they drown—they have a choice according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, because the two sides are equal. And Muhammad said: It is not permissible for them to throw themselves into the water, because that would be destruction by their own act. End quote.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a specific scenario: Muslims on a burning ship confronted with two equally deadly outcomes (burning or drowning). Two cases are distinguished: first, if they have a preponderant belief that jumping into the sea will allow them to swim to safety, they are obligated to jump to avoid certain death. Second, if staying and jumping both inevitably lead to death (no hope of escape), Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that they have a choice—they may stay and burn or jump and drown—because neither option is clearly better. However, Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that they may not jump, because actively throwing themselves into the water would be a direct act of self-destruction, which is not permissible. The dispute hinges on whether an act that leads to death but does not directly kill is considered 'killing oneself' (which is haram) vs. a permissible choice in an unavoidable situation.

Key Terms
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-ẓann): Preponderant belief or overwhelming probability – a standard for action when certainty is unattainable.
الخيار (al-khiyār): Option or choice – here, the permissibility to select either course of action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what is the ruling for Muslims on a burning ship when staying and jumping both lead to certain death?
A) They must stay and burn
B) They must jump into the water
C) They have a choice (khiyār)
D) It is haram to jump

Key Takeaway
When two outcomes are equally deadly, the majority Hanafi position gives a choice; the dissenting view (Muhammad) prohibits actively causing one's own death even when death is inevitable.

Taking the Quran and Women into a Sarīyah: The Hanafi Ruling on Dislike
Rulings on the prohibition of taking a Quran and women in a small military expedition (sarīyah) and related conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking a Mushaf and women into a sarīyah and distinguish the conditions under which it becomes disliked or permissible.

Core Idea
Taking a Mushaf or women into a small expedition is disliked to avoid desecration and fitnah, but not in a large army.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Rā'iq examines the ruling when a Muslim military detachment (sarīyah) enters enemy territory. The central question is whether it is permissible to bring a copy of the Quran (Mushaf) or women along. The answer depends on the size of the force, the age of the women, and the purpose. The passage clarifies the Hanafi position and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is taking a Mushaf and women into a sarīyah. The ruling is makruh tanzih (disliked) when there is fear for their safety.
The dislike is precautionary, not prohibitive.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'لا تسافروا بالقرآن في أرض العدو' (Do not travel with the Quran in enemy land), narrated by Bukhari and Muslim.
The hadith is mutawatir in meaning, but the ruling is derived as karahah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'do not travel' is understood as a recommendation (karahah tanzihiyyah) because the risk of desecration is not certain. The harm is potential, not actual.
The Hanafi usul principle: al-amr li al-nadbi when there is a countervailing risk.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Tahawi (within the Hanafi school) held that there is no dislike in any case. The author states the most correct and precautionary view is the dislike.
The disagreement is over the degree of risk and the interpretation of the hadith.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi position is that dislike applies in a sarīyah but not in a jaysh (large army) due to the prevailing safety. This is supported by the majority of Hanafi authorities.
The distinction between sarīyah and jaysh is key; various definitions are provided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ونهينا عن إخراج مصحف وامرأة في سرية يخاف عليها) لأن فيه تعريضهن على الضمان فلم يكن ذمة ويجب تقييده بما إذا لم يخف سوء عاقبة منه تأمل قوله : (بخلاف حالة المخمصة) قال في الفتح واعلم أن المذهب عندنا في المضطر أنه لا يجب عليه أكل مال الغير مع فرضاً فهو كالمباح يتقيد بشرط السلامة كالمرور في الطريق فلا حاجة إلى الفرق بينه وبين افتراض الجهاد في نفي الضمان .اهـ. أطلق المرأة فشمل الشابة والعجوز للمداواة أو غيرها كذا في الذخيرة. وقيد بالسرية لأنه لا كراهة في الإخراج إذا كان جيشاً يؤمن عليه لأن الغالب هو السلامة والغالب كالمتحقق. وفي المغرب: ولم يرد في تحديد السرية نص ومحصول ما ذكره محمد في السير أن التسعة وما فوقها سرية، وأما الأربعة والثلاثة ونحو ذلك طليعة لا سرية .اهـ. وفي الخانية قال أبو حنيفة : أقل السرية مائتان وأقل الجيش أربعمائة. وقال الحسن بن زياد : أقل السرية أربعمائة وأقل الجيش أربعة آلاف وفي المبسوط السرية عدد قليل يسيرون بالليل ويكمنون بالنهار اهـ. وفي فتح القدير : وينبغي كون العسكر العظيم اثني عشر ألفاً لما روي أنه عليه السلام قال لن تغلب اثناء عشر ألفاً من قلة وهو أكثر ما روي فيه اهـ. وظاهر مفهوم المختصر أن في الجيش لا يكره إخراج المرأة مطلقاً وخصوه بالعجائز للطب والمداواة والسقي، ويكره إخراج الشواب ولو احتيج إلى المباضعة فالأولى إخراج الإماء دون الحرائر والأولى عـدم إخراجهن أصلاً خوفاً من الفتن ولا تباشر المرأة القتال إلا عند الضرورة لأنه يستدل به على ضعفهم. وأراد بالمصحف ما يجب تعظيمه ويحرم الاستخفاف به فيكره إخراج كتب الفقه والحديث في سرية كما في فتح القدير. وقيد بالإخراج في السرية لأنه إذا دخل رجل مسلم إليهم بأمان لا بأس أن يحمل معه المصحف إذا كانوا قوماً يوفون بالعهد لأن الظاهر عدم التعرض. وفي الذخيرة قال محمد في أهل الثغور التي تلي أرض العدو: لا بأس أن يتخذوا فيها النساء وأن يكون لهم فيها الذراري وإن لم يكن بين تلك الثغور وبين أرض العدو أرض المسلمين إذا كان الرجال يقدرون على الدفع عنهم وإلا فلا ينبغي.
His statement: 'And we are forbidden from taking out a Mushaf and a woman in a sarīyah in which they are feared' – because in that there is exposing them to liability, so they would not have protection (dhimmah). And it must be restricted to when one does not fear a bad outcome from it. His statement: 'unlike the state of dire hunger' – said in Al-Fatḥ: And know that the school with us regarding the person compelled (muḍṭarr) is that it is not obligatory upon him to eat the property of another, even if it is fard? So it is like mubāḥ, conditioned by safety, like passing on the road. So there is no need to distinguish between it and the obligation of jihad in negating liability. End quote. He made woman general, including the young and old, for medicine or otherwise – thus in Al-Dhakhīrah. And it is restricted to sarīyah because there is no dislike if it is a jaysh (army) where safety is expected, because the prevailing condition is safety, and the prevailing is like the certain. In Al-Maghrib: No text has been transmitted for defining sarīyah, and the summary of what Muhammad mentioned in Al-Siyar is that nine and above is a sarīyah; as for four, three, and the like, that is a ṭalīʿah (scouting party), not a sarīyah. End quote. In Al-Khāniyyah: Abū Ḥanīfah said: The minimum of a sarīyah is two hundred, and the minimum of a jaysh is four hundred. And Al-Ḥasan ibn Ziyād said: The minimum of a sarīyah is four hundred, and the minimum of a jaysh is four thousand. In Al-Mabsūṭ: A sarīyah is a small number that travels by night and hides by day. End quote. In Fatḥ al-Qadīr: The great army should be twelve thousand, based on the narration that he ﷺ said: 'You will not be overcome by twelve thousand (who are) few in number' – and that is the most that has been narrated on it. End quote. And the apparent purport of the abridgment (al-mukhtaṣar) is that in the jaysh, taking out a woman is not disliked absolutely, and they specified it for old women for medicine, treatment, and giving drink. And taking out young women is disliked; and if there is need for sexual intercourse, it is better to take out slave women rather than free women, and best not to take them out at all for fear of fitnah. And the woman does not directly engage in fighting except in necessity, because it indicates their weakness. And by 'Mushaf' is meant what must be venerated and whose disrespect is prohibited; thus it is disliked to take out books of fiqh and hadith in a sarīyah, as in Fatḥ al-Qadīr. And it is restricted to taking out in a sarīyah because if a Muslim enters their land with amān, there is no harm in carrying the Mushaf with him if they are a people who fulfill covenants, because the apparent assumption is no harm. In Al-Dhakhīrah: Muḥammad said regarding the people of the frontiers (thughūr) adjacent to enemy territory: There is no harm in keeping women and having children there, even if there is no Muslim land between those frontiers and enemy land, so long as the men are able to defend them; otherwise, it is not appropriate.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive ruling on the prohibition and dislike of taking a Quran (Mushaf) and women into a small military expedition (sarīyah) where there is fear of harm. The commentary begins by explaining the reasoning: taking them exposes women to risk and the Mushaf to potential desecration. However, this ruling is restricted to sarīyah (small detachment) because in a large army (jaysh) where safety is expected, there is no dislike. Several definitions of sarīyah are given from different authorities: Muhammad al-Shaybānī (in Al-Siyar) says nine or more constitutes a sarīyah; Abū Ḥanīfah says two hundred; Al-Ḥasan ibn Ziyād says four hundred. The text also distinguishes between young and old women: old women may be taken for medical purposes, while young women are disliked due to fitnah. If sexual need arises, slave women (imā’) are preferred over free women. The Mushaf ruling extends to books of fiqh and hadith. However, if a Muslim enters with amān (safe conduct), he may carry the Mushaf if the people are trustworthy. The passage concludes with a ruling from Muḥammad about frontier posts: women and children may reside there if the men can defend them.

Key Terms
سرية (sariyyah): A small military detachment, usually sent at night for ambush or reconnaissance; size varies by opinion (from 9 to 400).
جيش (jaysh): A large army; in contrast to sarīyah, it is large enough to ensure safety.
مصحف (muṣḥaf): A physical codex of the Quran; also extended to other Islamic books.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is there no dislike (karāhah) for taking a woman into a military expedition?
A) If she is old and goes for medical treatment
B) If the expedition is a jaysh (large army) where safety is expected
C) If she is a slave and the need for sexual intercourse arises
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers it disliked (makruh tanzih) to take a Mushaf or women into a small military detachment (sarīyah) to avoid risk of desecration and fitnah, but not in a large army (jaysh) where safety is expected.
